Liability for Wall Collapse: Slave vs. Master
Ruling on liability for wall collapse by a slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for liability when a merchant slave's wall collapses, distinguishing between property and life loss, and explain the rational proof (ta'lil) that supports this distinction.

Core Idea
Property liability stays with the slave; life liability transfers to the master's aqilah — each assigned to the most suitable party.

The general principle of addressing a leaning wall applies even when the owner is a merchant slave. However, liability for resulting damage is split based on the nature of the loss. This passage explains the Hanafi position: property damage is borne by the slave (he may be sold to pay), while loss of life is borne by the master's aqilah. The proof is a rational assignment (ta'lil) based on which party is more fitting to bear each type of liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two types of loss
The passage concerns a merchant slave's wall that collapses after a notice to demolish. The loss is either property (mal) or life (nafs). Identify each as distinct cases.
The slave is the direct owner, but the master has overall responsibility.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each case
For property loss: liability is 'on the neck of the slave' meaning he must pay from his own value, even if sold. For life loss: blood money (diyah) is on the master's aqilah.
'Aqilah' refers to the master's clan who collectively pay diyah.
3
Extract the proof (ta'lil)
The text gives the reason: 'because the notice was partially on the master, and property liability is more befitting to the slave, and life liability to the master.' This is a rational argument (ta'lil) not a direct textual proof.
The proof is based on suitability (al-awlawiyyah) rather than a specific Quranic verse or hadith.
4
Understand the suitability rationale
Property damage is a financial matter; the slave has financial capacity (he may be sold) so it is suitable to hold him. Life liability involves blood money which is a social responsibility; the master's aqilah is better suited to bear it.
This reflects the Hanafi view that a slave has limited legal personality.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Other schools may not distinguish between property and life in this manner, or may place full liability on the master. This illustrates a Hanafi-specific ruling.
The proof's strength is zanni; it is not a qat'i ruling.

Source Text

ثم التالف بالسقوط إن كان مالا فهو في عنق العبد وإن كان نفسا فهو على عاقلة المولى لأن الإشهاد من جهة على المولى وضمان المال أليق بالعبد وضمان النفس بالمولى ترجمہ پھر ہلاک ہونے والا اگر مال ہوتو وہ غلام کی گردن میں ہو گا اور اگر نفس ہو تو وہ آقا کے عاقلہ پر ہوگا اس لئے کہ اشہاد من وجہ آقا پر ہے اور مال کا ضمان غلام کے زیادہ لائق ہے اور نفس کا ضمان مولی کے زیادہ لائق ہے۔ تشریح اگر غلام تاجر کی دیوار تھی اور اس سے توڑنے کا مطالبہ کیا گیا تو یہ مطالبہ من وجہ غلام سے ہے اور من وجہ آقا ء سے تو دونوں کی رعایت رکھی گئی اور کہا گیا کہ جو چیز ہلاک ہوئی اور وہ مال ہے تو اس کی ادائیگی غلام کی گردن سے ہو گی ۔ یہاں تک کہ اس کو فروخت کر کے قرض ادا کیا جائے گا اور اگر ہلاک شدہ کوئی آدمی ہو تو اس کی دیت آقاء کے عاقلہ پر واجب ہوگی کیونکہ مال کا منمان غلام پر واجب کرنا مناسب ہے اور نفس کا منمان آقا پر واجب کرنا مناسب ہے۔
Then, what is destroyed by the fall: if it is property, it is on the neck of the slave; if it is a life, it is on the aqilah of the master, because the notice was [served] partially on the master, and the liability for property is more befitting to the slave, and the liability for life is [more befitting] to the master. Translation: Then, the destroyed thing: if it is property, it is on the slave's neck; if it is a life, it is on the master's aqilah, because the notice is partially on the master, and property liability is more appropriate for the slave, and life liability is more appropriate for the master. Commentary: If a merchant slave's wall was [leaning] and he was demanded to demolish, then the demand is partially from the slave and partially from the master, so both are considered. It is said: if what is destroyed is property, its payment is from the slave's neck (he may be sold to pay the debt); if it is a person, then his blood money is wajib on the master's aqilah, because imposing property liability on the slave is suitable, and imposing life liability on the master is suitable.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage provides a specific ruling within the general principle of addressing demands to demolish walls. It deals with the case of a merchant slave (ghulam tajir) who owns a wall. When the wall collapses, liability is split between the slave and his master depending on the nature of the damage. The Arabic matn states that if property is destroyed, the liability (daman) is 'on the neck of the slave' meaning he must pay from his own value, even if that requires selling him. If a person is killed, the blood money (diyah) is on the master's aqilah (the group responsible for paying blood money on his behalf). The reasoning: the legal notice (ishhad) is partially directed to the master (since the slave is his property), and it is more appropriate to assign property liability to the slave (who has some financial capacity) and life liability to the master (who has a broader responsibility for the slave's actions). The commentary explains that the demand was made considering both parties, and the distinction ensures that the liability falls on the most suitable party. This reflects the Hanafi approach to balancing the slave's limited legal personality with the master's overall responsibility.

Key Terms
عاقلة (aqilah): The group of male agnate relatives (or tribe) responsible for paying blood money (diyah) on behalf of the killer.
في عنق العبد (fi 'unuq al-'abd): Liability on the slave's 'neck' meaning he must personally bear the cost, even if it means he must be sold to raise the funds.
من جهة (min jihah): Partially, from one aspect. The demand or notice is considered partially directed to the master because the slave is his property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is liability for property damage placed on the slave and liability for loss of life on the master's aqilah?
A) Because the slave committed a sin by not demolishing the wall.
B) Because the notice was given to the master alone.
C) Because property liability is more befitting to the slave and life liability to the master.
D) Because the master is richer than the slave.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a merchant slave's wall collapses, liability is split: property damage is on the slave (he may be sold), loss of life is on the master's aqilah, based on the suitability of each party to bear that type of liability.

Demanding Demolition of a Dangerous Wall
Lists persons with authority to demolish and establishes liability rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the categories of persons from whom demolition of a leaning wall can be demanded and determine liability for resulting harm according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Only those with legal authority to demolish can be demanded; liability follows the capable party.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah establishes a key principle in liability for dangerous walls: a formal demand to demolish can only be made against those who have both the physical ability and legal authority to do so. The author then lists six specific categories of persons who meet this condition and explains how liability for collapse is assigned, distinguishing between property damage and loss of life. Understanding these rules is essential for applying the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' in property and injury cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle
Recognize that the author states a foundational rule: only a person who is able to demolish the wall (physically and legally) can be validly demanded to do so. Those without ability or authority cannot be held responsible.
This principle limits liability to capable parties.
2
List the Persons with Authority
From the passage, identify the six categories: mortgagee (murtahin), executor (wasi), orphan's paternal grandfather (jadd), orphan's mother (from Ziyadat), mukatab (a slave under contract of manumission), and trading slave (tajir).
The mother's inclusion is specifically attributed to Imam Muhammad in Ziyadat, indicating an intra-Hanafi variant.
3
Understand Liability for Orphan's Wall
If the wall belongs to an orphan and the guardians were demanded but did not demolish it, and it collapses causing harm, the liability is on the orphan's property because the guardians' inaction is considered equivalent to the orphan's own act.
This follows the principle that a guardian's act in managing property is attributed to the minor.
4
Differentiate Liability for Trading Slave's Wall
For a trading slave's wall, the demand is made partially on the slave and partially on the master. If the collapse causes property damage, the slave must pay (even by being sold); if it causes death, blood money (diyah) is on the master's aqila (paternal relatives).
This distinction is based on suitability: property liability is more fitting for the slave engaged in trade, while life liability is more appropriate for the master.
5
Apply to a Scenario
Take a scenario: a trading slave's wall collapses and kills a pedestrian. Determine that the blood money is wajib on the master's aqila, not on the slave, because the loss is a life (nafs), not property.
This step reinforces the practical application of the ruling.

Executor, orphan's grandfather – here grandfather means paternal grandfather, because if father is there, he would not be an orphan. Orphan's mother because she has guardianship over the child's wall, but Imam Muhammad mentioned the mother in Ziyadat. -5 Mukatab: if it is the mukatab's wall, demanding from him to break is valid because he has the authority to break. Trading slave: whether he has debt or not, because the slave has the authority to break. Answer: In the orphan's property because the guardians' act is like the orphan's act. If it was a trading slave's wall and he was demanded to break it, from whom is the demand, the slave or the master? Commentary: If it was a trading slave's wall and he was demanded to break it, the demand is partially from the slave and partially from the master, so both are considered. It was said: if what is destroyed is property, its payment is from the slave's neck, to the extent that he will be sold to pay the debt. If the destroyed item is a human, then his blood money is wajib on the master's aqila because imposing property liability on the slave is appropriate and imposing life liability on the master is appropriate.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah discusses the ruling on demanding the demolition of a leaning wall that poses danger. The author first states a general principle: only those who have the ability and legal authority to demolish the wall can be validly demanded to do so. Then he lists six categories of persons who have such authority: (1) the mortgagee (murtahin), because he can redeem the pledged property and repair it; (2) the executor (wasi) of an orphan's property; (3) the orphan's paternal grandfather (jadd), provided the father is deceased; (4) the orphan's mother, as mentioned by Imam Muhammad in al-Ziyadat; (5) the mukatab (a slave under contract of manumission), for his own wall; and (6) a trading slave (tajir) who is authorized to trade, regardless of debt. The passage then addresses a scenario: if the wall belongs to an orphan and the guardians were already demanded but did not demolish it, and the wall collapses killing someone, the liability (daman) is on the orphan's property because the guardians' inaction is attributed to the orphan. For a trading slave's wall, the demand is made partially on the slave and partially on the master. If the falling object causes property damage, the slave must pay from his own means (even if sold); if it causes death, the blood money (diyah) is on the master's aqila (tribal guarantors). This distinction is based on fittingness: property liability is more appropriate for the slave, who is engaged in trade, while life liability falls on the master, who has higher status.

Key Terms
تقدم (taqaddum): Demand or legal request to demolish a dangerous wall.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for loss or damage.
عاقلة (a'qila): The group of male agnatic relatives responsible for paying blood money in cases of accidental killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The slave's aqila
D) The master's aqila

Key Takeaway
Only those with legal authority can be demanded to demolish a dangerous wall, and liability for collapse depends on who is capable and the type of loss.

Demand Against One Co-Heir for Leaning Wall
Ruling on demanding one co-owner to demolish a shared leaning wall

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on demanding a single co-heir to repair his share of a leaning wall and explain the method of enforcement through the judge.

Core Idea
A single co-heir can be validly demanded to repair his share of a leaning wall, even if alone, by seeking judicial help.

When a wall jointly owned by heirs is leaning, one might think the demand must be made to all. But Hanafi law permits demanding a single co-heir for his portion, as he can compel others via the judge. This lesson explains the ruling, the method of judicial recourse, and the extent of liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize the case: multiple heirs inherit a house with a leaning wall. One heir is demanded to demolish or repair his share.
The wall is shared property, not individually owned.
2
State the Ruling
The demand (taqaddum) against one heir for his share is valid (sahih). Even if he cannot demolish alone, he has a method.
Validity is based on ability to enforce via judge.
3
Explain the Method
The method is murafa'ah: raising the matter to the judge. The judge can then order the other co-heirs to cooperate.
This ensures the demand is not futile.
4
Determine Liability
The demanded heir is liable only for his own share, not the shares of others. If the wall collapses due to delay, he is only responsible for his portion.
Liability is proportional to ownership.
5
Justify the Ruling
The reason liability is limited to his share is because the demand was validly made against him for his share only.
The ruling follows logically from the validity of the demand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويصح التقدم إلى أحد الورثة فى نصيبه وإن كان لا يتمكن من نقض الحائط وحده لتمكنه من إصلاح نصيبه بطريقه وهو المرافعة إلى القاضي.
ترجمہ اور تقدم صحیح ہے ورثا میں سے ایک کی جانب اس کے حصہ میں اگر چہ وہ تنہا اس کے توڑنے پر قدرت نہیں رکھتا اس کے قادر ہونے کی وجہ سے اپنے حصہ کی اصلاح پر اصلاح کے طریقہ کے ساتھ اور وہ طریقہ قاضی کی طرف مرافعہ ہے۔ تشریح ایک مکان چند شخصوں کو میراث میں ملا اور اس مکان کی دیوار جھکی ہوئی ہے تو وہ سب مل کر اس کی مرمت کرتے ہیں اور اگر ان میں سے ایک مرمت کرنا چاہے تو وہ بھی کر سکتا ہے جس کا طریقہ یہ ہوگا کہ قاضی کے سامنے مسئلہ رکھدے تو قاضی بقیہ شرکاء کو اس کی مرمت کا حکم دیدے گا لہذا اگر توڑنے کا مطالبہ ورثامیں سے صرف ایک سے کیا گیا تو صحیح ہے۔
لیکن وہ ایک وارث صرف اپنے حصہ کا ضامن ہو گا باقیوں کے حصہ کا ضامن نہ ہوگا اور اپنے حصہ کا ضامن کیوں ہوگا ؟ تو اس کی وجہ یہی ہے کہ اس کی جانب تقدم درست ہے۔ ( کمامز )
And it is valid to make a demand (taqaddum) to one of the heirs regarding his share, even if he is not able to demolish the wall alone, because he is able to repair his share by his method, and that is raising the matter to the judge. Translation: And the demand is valid towards one of the heirs in his share, even if he alone is not capable of demolishing it, due to his ability to repair his share by his method, and that method is raising the matter to the judge. Commentary: A house is inherited by several people and its wall is leaning, so they all jointly repair it. If one of them wants to repair, he can do so by presenting the case to the judge, and the judge will order the other partners to repair. Therefore, if the demand to demolish is made to only one of the heirs, it is valid. But that one heir will only be liable for his own share, not for the shares of the others. And why is he liable for his own share? Because the demand against him is valid. (Kamamaz?)
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah clarifies the ruling on demanding the demolition of a leaning wall that is jointly owned by multiple heirs. The text states that it is valid to make the demand to a single co-heir regarding his own share, even if he cannot demolish the entire wall by himself. The reason is that he has the ability to repair his share through legal means: he can take the matter to the judge, who can then compel the other co-owners to cooperate in the repair. Thus, the demand is valid and the individual heir is liable for his own portion only. The passage also notes that if the wall collapses due to delay, the one who was demanded is only responsible for his own share of the damage, not for the others' shares. This follows logically from the validity of the demand against him. The ruling emphasizes that joint ownership does not prevent individual accountability when a specific party has the authority to act.

Key Terms
مرافعة (murafa'ah): Raising a case to the judge for a ruling or order.
نقض (naqd): Demolition or breaking down of a wall.

Liability for Second Victim from Collapsed Wall
Distinguishing liability based on cause of second death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between two scenarios of a second death after a wall collapse and apply the Hanafi rule on liability based on causation.

Core Idea
Liability for a second death depends on the cause: tripping over a corpse does not create liability; falling debris does.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah examines a nuanced tort scenario. A leaning wall collapses after the owner has been notified (ishhad), killing one person. A second person then dies either by tripping over the first victim's corpse or by being struck by debris. The Hanafi ruling hinges on the duty to clear the path and the ownership of the debris.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the text and separate the two ways the second person dies: (a) by tripping over the corpse of the first victim, (b) by being struck or slipping on debris from the wall.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For scenario (a), the wall owner is not liable for the second death. For scenario (b), the owner is liable.
3
Analyze the reasoning
The owner is not liable in (a) because clearing the corpse is the duty of the first victim's heirs. He is liable in (b) because the debris is his property and he must clear it; notification about the wall also applies to the debris.
4
Apply the rule to a new case
Consider a modern case: a notified wall collapses, kills a pedestrian. A second pedestrian trips over the corpse and dies. Apply the rule and explain why the owner is not liable for the second death.

Source Text

ترجمہ اور اگر جھکی ہوئی دیوار اشہاد کے بعد کسی انسان پر گر گئی پس اس کو قتل کر دیا پس مقتول کے ساتھ اس کا پیر پھسل گیا پس ہلاک ہو گیا تو وہ اس کا ضامن نہ ہو گا اس لئے کہ اس سے راستہ کو خالی کرنا اس کے اولیاء کا کام ہے نہ کہ اس کا ۔ تشریح زید کی دیوار جھکی ہوئی تھی اس کے گرنے سے خالد مر گیا اور خالد سے پھسل کر بکر مر گیا تو بکر کا ضمان زید کے اوپر نہ ہوگا۔

And if the leaning wall falls on a person after the notification and kills him, then another person stumbles over the corpse and dies, he (the wall owner) is not liable for him because clearing (the path) is the duty of the heirs (of the first victim), not his. Translation: And if a leaning wall falls on a person after notification and kills him, then another person stumbles over the corpse and dies, he (the owner) is not liable for him because clearing the path is the duty of the heirs (of the deceased), not his. Commentary: Zaid's wall was leaning. It fell on Khalid and killed him. Then Bakr tripped over Khalid and died. Bakr's liability is not upon Zaid because removing Khalid is the duty of Khalid's heirs, not Zaid's. So only Khalid's liability is on Zaid, not Bakr's. And if the second person dies due to debris, then he is liable. And if he is destroyed by the debris, he is liable for him because clearing (the debris) is upon him (the owner), since the debris is his property, and the notification about the wall is a notification about the debris because the purpose is to prevent obstruction. Translation: And if he (Bakr) died due to the debris, then he (Zaid) is liable for him because clearing is upon him (Zaid), since the debris is his property, and the notification about the wall is a notification about the debris because the purpose is to prevent obstruction. Commentary: If Bakr, instead of tripping over Khalid, slipped on the debris of Zaid's wall, then Bakr's liability is also upon Zaid because clearing the debris is Zaid's duty, since the debris belongs to him.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah examines a scenario where a leaning wall collapses after the owner has been notified and kills one person. A second person then dies in one of two ways: by tripping over the first victim's corpse, or by being hit by falling debris. The ruling distinguishes between them. For the second death caused by tripping over the corpse, the wall owner is not liable because the duty to remove the corpse falls on the heirs of the first victim, not on the owner. The owner is only liable for the first death caused directly by the wall. However, if the second person dies due to debris (e.g., slipping on pieces of the wall or being struck by them), the owner is liable. The reasoning is that the debris is the owner's property, and the notification (ishhad) about the wall also applies to the debris, as the purpose of the notification is to prevent obstruction and harm. Therefore, clearing the debris is the owner's responsibility, and any resulting death is attributable to his negligence. This distinction shows the importance of causation and duty in establishing liability in Hanafi tort law.

Key Terms
تفريغ (tafrigh): Clearing or removing an obstruction from a path.
نقض (naqd): Debris or pieces of a demolished structure.
إشهاد (ishhad): Notification or formal warning given about a dangerous wall.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Adam and Eve
B) Adam and Bill
C) Eve and Bill
D) Only Adam

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi tort law, causation and duty assignment determine liability for second deaths after a wall collapse.

Repair of a Leaning Wall by Co-Heirs
Ruling on repair of a leaning wall in inherited property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on repair of a leaning wall inherited by multiple heirs and explain the legal method (murafa'ah) that allows a single heir to compel others.

Core Idea
A single co-owner can demand his share of repair via the judge, and is only liable for his own portion.

Inheritance often creates joint ownership, and with that comes shared responsibility for maintenance. But what if one heir wants to act and others refuse? This passage from Hidayah addresses exactly that: a leaning wall in an inherited house. The Hanafi position provides a practical solution through judicial recourse, ensuring that repair obligations can be enforced without requiring unanimous consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal problem
Recognize the scenario: a house inherited by multiple heirs, with a leaning wall that requires repair. The key issue is who is obligated and how to enforce participation.
2
Determine the obligation
The text implies that repair is wajib upon all co-owners. It states that if one heir wishes to repair, he can, indicating that it is a shared responsibility.
Hanafi: wajib, not fard, because the evidence is based on qiyas and istihsan.
3
Understand the method of enforcement
A single heir can approach the judge (murafa'ah ila al-qadi), who can order the other partners to cooperate. This is the legal avenue for an individual to compel collective action.
The text emphasizes that even if he cannot physically demolish the wall alone, he has recourse through the court.
4
Analyze the liability
If an heir proceeds alone or is approached individually, he is only liable for his own share of the wall, not for the shares of others. This is because the demand (taqaddum) was made validly towards him.
The reasoning: since he can be compelled, he is responsible for his portion.
5
Note the underlying reasoning
The ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) and qiyas khafi (hidden analogy). The ability to seek judicial intervention is treated as equivalent to physical ability, preventing injustice.
This shows the practical flexibility of Hanafi fiqh in property disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ایک مکان چند شخصوں کو میراث میں ملا، اس کی جھکی ہوئی دیوار کی مرمت کون کرے گا؟
ويصح التقدم إلى أحد الورثة فى نصيبه وإن كان لا يتمكن من نقض الحائط وحده لتمكنه من إصلاح نصيبه بطريقه وهو المرافعة إلى القاضي.
ترجمہ اور تقدم صحیح ہے ورثا میں سے ایک کی جانب اس کے حصہ میں اگر چہ وہ تنہا اس کے توڑنے پر قدرت نہیں رکھتا اس کے قادر ہونے کی وجہ سے اپنے حصہ کی اصلاح پر اصلاح کے طریقہ کے ساتھ اور وہ طریقہ قاضی کی طرف مرافعہ ہے۔ تشریح ایک مکان چند شخصوں کو میراث میں ملا اور اس مکان کی دیوار جھکی ہوئی ہے تو وہ سب مل کر اس کی مرمت کرتے ہیں اور اگر ان میں سے ایک مرمت کرنا چاہے تو وہ بھی کر سکتا ہے جس کا طریقہ یہ ہوگا کہ قاضی کے سامنے مسئلہ رکھدے تو قاضی بقیہ شرکاء کو اس کی مرمت کا حکم دیدے گا لہذا اگر توڑنے کا مطالبہ ورثامیں سے صرف ایک سے کیا گیا تو صحیح ہے۔ لیکن وہ ایک وارث صرف اپنے حصہ کا ضامن ہو گا باقیوں کے حصہ کا ضامن نہ ہوگا اور اپنے حصہ کا ضامن کیوں ہوگا ؟ تو اس کی وجہ یہی ہے کہ اس کی جانب تقدم درست ہے۔
A house was inherited by several people. Who will repair its leaning wall? And it is valid to approach one of the heirs regarding his share, even if he is unable to demolish the wall alone, because he is able to repair his share by his method, which is referring the matter to the judge. Translation and explanation: The approach is valid toward one of the heirs regarding his share, even if he alone is not able to demolish it, due to his ability to repair his share by his method, and that method is referral to the judge. Elaboration: A house was inherited by several people and its wall is leaning; then they all together repair it. If one of them wishes to repair, he can do so. His method is that he presents the matter before the judge, and the judge orders the remaining partners to repair it. Hence, if demolition is demanded from only one of the heirs, it is valid. But that one heir is only liable for his share, not for the shares of others. And why is he liable for his share? The reason is that the approach toward him is valid.

Commentary
This passage addresses a practical scenario: a leaning wall in a house inherited by multiple heirs. The ruling clarifies that each heir is responsible for his portion, but one heir can compel the others to repair by taking the case to a judge. The Hanafi position is that the warning (ishhad) or demand to demolish can be made against a single heir, and he is liable only for his own share of any damage. The reasoning is based on istihsan: although he cannot act alone physically, he has legal recourse through the court, which can order participation. This prevents injustice.

Key Terms
تقدم (taqaddum): Legal approach or demand made to a specific heir to repair his share of the wall.
مرافعة (murafa'ah): Referring the dispute to a judge for a binding order.

Liability for Second Victim Tripping on Corpse
Ruling on liability for second death after tripping on corpse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a tortfeasor's liability is severed when a resulting duty shifts to another party, and determine who bears responsibility for subsequent harm.

Core Idea
The wall owner owes diyah only for the first death; the heirs must clear the body or face liability.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in Hanafi tort law: a leaning wall collapses and kills one person; a second person then trips over the corpse and dies. Is the wall owner liable for both deaths? The text explains that the duty to remove the obstruction transfers to the first victim's heirs, thereby cutting off the original tortfeasor's causal connection. Understanding this principle clarifies how liability chains are broken in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sequence of events
Note that there are two separate deaths: the first caused directly by the wall, the second caused by tripping over the first victim's body. The wall owner's act is the initial cause, but a new cause (failure to clear) intervenes.
The passage assumes the wall owner had issued a public warning (ishhad) before the collapse.
2
Determine liability for the first victim
The wall owner is liable for the first death because his property directly caused the harm. The diyah (blood money) is due from him, as per the default rule for owners of dangerous structures.
No dispute: the first victim's heirs can claim diyah from the wall owner.
3
Analyze the second death
The question is whether the second death is also attributable to the wall owner. The text rules that it is not, because the duty to clear the corpse (tafriqh) falls on the first victim's heirs (awliya'), not on the wall owner.
The reasoning: after the first death, the wall owner's responsibility ends; the heirs must remove the body in a timely manner.
4
Understand the proof (dalil)
The proof is based on the effective cause ('illa): the duty of tafriqh shifts to the heirs, thus severing the wall owner's causal link. This is an example of qiyas khafi (obscure analogy) or istihsan? Actually the text states it as a straightforward ruling. The dalil is the transmission of duty.
The Arabic phrase 'لأن التفريغ إلى الأولياء لا إليه' is the key: 'because clearing is for the heirs, not for him'.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, if a similar chain of events occurs, the original tortfeasor is only liable for the first harm. Subsequent harms arising from others' failure to fulfill their duties are borne by those who neglected their obligation.
This principle can be extended to other contexts, such as a driver hitting a pedestrian and then another car hitting the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دیوار کے گرنے سے ایک شخص فوت ہو گیا دوسرا مقتول کے ساتھ پھسل کر ہلاک ہو گیا تو دوسرے کا ضمان مالک دیوار پر نہ ہوگا
ولو سقط الحائل المائل على إنسان بعد الإشهاد فقتله فتعثر بالقتيل غيره فعطب لا يضمنه لأن التفريغ إلى الأولياء لا إليه
ترجمہ اور اگر جھکی ہوئی دیوار اشہاد کے بعد کسی انسان پر گر گئی پس اس کو قتل کر دیا پس مقتول کے ساتھ اس کا پیر پھسل گیا پس ہلاک ہو گیا تو وہ اس کا ضامن نہ ہو گا اس لئے کہ اس سے راستہ کو خالی کرنا اس کے اولیاء کا کام ہے نہ کہ اس کا ۔ تشریح زید کی دیوار جھکی ہوئی تھی اس کے گرنے سے خالد مر گیا اور خالد سے پھسل کر بکر مر گیا تو بکر کا ضمان زید کے اوپر نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ خالد کا راستہ سے اٹھانا خالد کے اولیاء کا کام ہے نہ کہ زید کا تو اس پر صرف خالد کا ضمان ہوگا بکر کا نہ ہوگا۔
A person died due to the collapse of the wall, another slipped on the victim and died; then the second's liability is not upon the wall owner. And if a leaning wall fell upon a person after warning and killed him, then another person stumbled over the victim and died, he (the wall owner) is not liable for him, because clearing the path is the duty of the victim's heirs, not his. Translation and explanation: If Zaid's leaning wall fell and killed Khalid, and then Bakr slipped on Khalid and died, Zaid is not liable for Bakr. Because removing Khalid from the path is the duty of Khalid's heirs, not Zaid's. So Zaid is only liable for Khalid, not for Bakr.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents a scenario where a leaning wall collapses after due warning (ishhad), killing one person. A second person then trips over the corpse and dies. The ruling is that the wall owner is liable only for the first victim. The reasoning is that once the first person is killed, the obligation to clear the body from the path falls on his heirs (awliya'), not on the wall owner. Therefore, the second death is not directly caused by the wall owner's negligence but by the failure of the heirs to remove the corpse. The owner is thus not liable for the second death.

Key Terms
تفريغ (tafriqh): Clearing the path of an obstruction, here the corpse.
أولياء (awliyā'): Heirs or legal representatives of the deceased, responsible for burial and clearing the body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The wall owner, because his wall caused the sequence of events.

C) Amr himself, for not looking where he was going.
D) No one is liable.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a tortfeasor's liability for subsequent harm is cut off when the duty to mitigate shifts to another party, such as the first victim's heirs.

Liability for Death Caused by Debris of Wall
Ruling when second person dies due to debris of the wall, not tripping on corpse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a death is caused by the debris of a wall and explain why the warning on the wall legally extends to its debris.

Core Idea
A wall owner's liability for death caused by his wall's debris follows from the fact that the debris is his property and the warning covers all his property's obstructions.

In the previous lesson, we examined the case where a passerby trips over a corpse and dies—the owner of the wall was not liable for that death. Now we consider a different scenario: the second person dies not from tripping over a person, but from slipping on the debris of the wall itself. Here, the ruling changes. The wall owner is held liable. Why? This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning linking property ownership and the scope of the formal warning (ishhad).

Guided Path
1
Identify the causative agent
The death is caused by the debris (naqd) of the wall, not by another person. This is the key factual distinction from the previous case.
2
Determine ownership
The debris is property of the wall owner (النقض ملكه). Since he owns it, he has a duty to prevent it from obstructing the public path.
Ownership is the basis of the duty to clear (tafriqh).
3
Link the warning to the debris
The formal warning (ishhad) given on the wall is legally considered a warning on its debris because the purpose of the warning is to prevent any obstruction caused by the owner's property, not just the wall standing.
The maqsad (purpose) is to avoid shughl (obstruction) of the path.
4
Conclude liability
Because the owner failed to clear his property after warning, he becomes liable (damān) for the death caused by the debris.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اگر دوسرا شخص کسی ٹوٹن کی وجہ سے ہلاک ہوا تو ضمان ہوگا
وإن عطب بالنقض ضمنه لأن التفريغ إليه إذ النقض ملكه والإشهاد على الحائط إشهاد على النقض لأن المقصود امتناع الشغل.
ترجمہ اور اگر وہ (بکر) ٹوٹن کی وجہ سے ہلاک ہوا ہو تو وہ اس کا ضامن ہو گا اس لئے کہ خالی کرنا اس کا ( زید کا کام ہے اس لئے کہ ٹوٹن اس کی ملک ہے اور دیوار اشہاد ٹوٹن پر اشہاد ہے اس لئے کہ مقصود مشغولیت سے باز رہنا ہے۔
تشریح اگر بکر بجائے خالد سے پھسلنے کے زید کی دیوار کی ٹوٹن سے پھسلا ہو تو پھر بکر کا ضمان بھی زید کے اوپر ہو گا۔ اس لئے کہ ٹوٹن سے راستہ کو صاف کرنا زید کا فریضہ ہے کیونکہ ٹوٹن کا طالب زید ہی ہے۔
سوال . اشہاد تو فقط دیوار پر تھا نہ کہ ٹوٹن پر تو پھر ضمان کیسا؟
جواب ٹوٹن پر اشہاد ہی ٹوٹن پر اشہاد ہے، اس لئے کہ مقصود تو یہ ہے کہ اس کی ملک راستہ کو نہ گھیرے۔ اور یہاں اس کی ملک نے راستہ کو گھیر رکھا ہے۔
If the second person died due to the debris (of the wall), then there is liability. And if he died due to the debris, he (the owner) is liable for him, because clearing it is his duty, since the debris is his property, and the warning regarding the wall is a warning regarding the debris, because the purpose is to prevent obstruction. Translation: And if he (Bakr) died due to the debris, then he (Zaid) is liable for him, because clearing is Zaid's duty, since the debris is his property, and the warning on the wall is a warning on the debris, because the purpose is to avoid obstruction. Elaboration: If Bakr slipped on the debris of Zaid's wall instead of on Khalid, then Zaid is liable for Bakr as well, because clearing the debris from the path is Zaid's obligation, since the debris belongs to Zaid. Question: The warning was only on the wall, not on the debris; then how is there liability? Answer: The warning on the wall is necessarily a warning on the debris, because the purpose is that his property should not obstruct the path, and here his property does obstruct the path.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage contrasts with the previous one. Here, the second death is caused directly by the debris (النقض) of the wall, not by tripping over the first victim. The ruling is that the wall owner is liable for the second death as well. The reasoning is that the debris is his property (ملكه), and the warning (ishhad) given on the wall applies equally to its debris because the purpose of the warning is to prevent obstruction of the public path. Since the owner failed to clear his property, he is liable for any resulting death. The commentary also addresses a potential objection: the warning was only on the wall, not the debris. The answer is that warning on the wall implies warning on its debris because the debris is an extension of the wall's danger.

Key Terms
نقض (naqd): Debris or falling pieces of the wall, owned by the wall owner.
إشهاد (ishhad): Formal warning or testimony given to the wall owner about the danger.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the wall owner liable for a death caused by the debris of his wall after warning?
A) Because the debris is considered his property and the warning on the wall applies to it.
B) Because the debris is a separate object not covered by the warning.
C) Because the debris belongs to the public.
D) Because the owner intended harm.

Key Takeaway
Liability for death by debris follows from property ownership and the comprehensive scope of the formal warning.

Liability for a Pot on a Wall Causing Death
Ruling on liability with ownership-based distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a pot on a wall falls and causes death, and apply the ownership-based distinction to determine who is liable.

Core Idea
Liability follows ownership of the dangerous object, not just the location.

In Hanafi law, when an object causes harm, liability is tied to the duty to remove the danger (tafriqh). This lesson examines a case where a collapsing wall has a pot on it. The wall kills one person, and the pot kills another. The ruling hinges on who owns the pot, because clearing it is the owner's responsibility. Understanding this distinction clarifies how Hanafi fiqh assigns liability in multi‑cause accidents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Read the passage and note the two deaths: one by the wall collapsing, one by the pot falling (or causing a slip). The key variable is ownership of the pot.
The wall owner is always liable for the wall's collapse.
2
Apply the ownership distinction
If the pot belongs to the wall owner, he is liable for both deaths. If the pot belongs to someone else, the pot owner is liable for the death caused by the pot.
3
Extract the legal reasoning (dalil)
The reason (ta'lil) given is 'al-tafriqh ila malikiha' – the duty to clear the obstruction lies with the owner of the object. This is a qiyās khafī based on general principles of liability.
The proof is aqli (rational), not a direct text from Qur'an or Sunnah.
4
Distinguish from other scenarios
Note that if the pot owner had not placed the pot on that wall, liability might differ. The ruling assumes the pot was placed with permission or is simply there.
Absence of negligence by the wall owner is assumed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
گرنے والی دیوار پرگھڑ رکھا تھا اور گھڑا بھی مالک مکان کا تھا اس سے کوئی ہلاک ہو گیا مالک دار ضامن ہوگا
ولو عطب بجرة كانت على الحائط فسقطت بسقوطه وهى ملكه ضمنه لأن التفريغ إليه وإن كان ملك غيره لا يضمنه لأن التفريغ إلى مالكها
ترجمہ اور اگر وہ ( بکر) اس گھڑے سے پھسلا ہو جو دیوار پر تھا پس دیوار کے گرنے سے وہ گر گیا، حالانکہ وہ گھڑا اس کی (زید کی ) ملکیت ہے تو وہ (زید) اس کا ضامن ہو گا اس لئے کہ تفریغ اس کا کام ہے اور اگر اس کے غیر کی ملک ہو تو ضامن نہ ہو گا اس لئے کہ تفریغ اسکے مالک کی جانب ہے۔ تشریح زید کی جھکی ہوئی دیوار پر مثلاً کوئی شیرہ کا گھڑا رکھا ہے اور دیوار گری جس سے خالد ہلاک ہو گیا تو اس کا ضمان زید کے اوپر ہے۔ اور دیوار کے گرنے سے گھڑا بھی گرا اب اس میں پھسل کر بکر مر گیا تو بکر کا ضمان کس پر ہو گا ؟ تو فرمایا کہ اگر کھڑے کا مالک زید ہی ہے تو منمان زید پر واجب ہوگا اور اگر اس کی دیوار پر کسی اور نے سکھانے کے لئے اپنا گھڑارکھ دیا تو منمان بکر گھڑے کے مالک پر لازم ہوگا۔ وجہ اس کی یہ ہے کہ جو گھڑے کا مالک ہو گا اس کو راستہ میں سے اٹھانا اور راستہ کو صاف کرنا اس کا فریضہ ہے لہذا پہلی صورت میں یہ زید کا فریضہ ہے اور دوسری صورت میں اس کا فریضہ ہے جس کا یہ گھڑا ہے۔
On the collapsing wall there was a pot, and the pot belonged to the house owner. Someone was killed by it, so the house owner is liable. And if he (Bakr) slipped on a pot that was on the wall and fell when the wall fell, and it was his (Zaid's) property, then he (Zaid) is liable for him, because clearing (the path) is his duty. But if it was the property of someone else, he is not liable, because clearing is the duty of its owner. Translation: And if he (Bakr) slipped on the pot that was on the wall and fell due to the wall's collapse, and that pot is Zaid's property, then Zaid is liable for him, because clearing is his duty. If it was the property of another, then he is not liable, because clearing is the duty of its owner. Elaboration: For example, on Zaid's leaning wall there is a pot of molasses. The wall collapses and kills Khalid; Zaid is liable for that. Then the pot also falls, and Bakr slips on it and dies. Who is liable for Bakr? It is said: if the pot is Zaid's property, then liability is on Zaid; if someone else placed his pot on the wall to dry, then liability for Bakr is on the pot's owner. The reason is that the owner of the pot must clear it from the path. So in the first case it is Zaid's duty, and in the second it is the duty of the one who owns the pot.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage deals with a pot (jar) placed on a leaning wall. When the wall falls, the pot also falls and causes a second death (by tripping or impact). The ruling distinguishes based on ownership of the pot. If the pot belongs to the wall owner, he is liable for both deaths (the wall and the pot). If the pot belongs to someone else, the wall owner is only liable for the death caused by the wall; the pot's owner is liable for the death caused by the pot. The reasoning is that the duty to clear (tafriqh) falls on the owner of the object that obstructs the path. Thus, each owner is responsible for his own property's obstruction.

Key Terms
جرة (jarrah): A pot or large jug placed on the wall.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person places his pot on another's leaning wall, the wall collapses and kills someone by the pot, who is liable for that death according to this passage?
A) The wall owner.
B) The pot owner.
C) Both equally.
D) Neither, as it was an accident.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, liability for harm caused by an object on another's property follows ownership of the object, not ownership of the property.

Liability of a Single Partner among Five for Wall Collapse
Ruling when only one of five partners is warned and wall kills someone

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for a jointly-owned dangerous structure when only one partner is warned, and explain why the liability is proportional and falls on the aqila.

Core Idea
Liability for a shared danger attaches only to the warned partner and only to the extent of his ownership.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in the Hanafi law of diyat (blood money): a wall owned by five partners collapses and kills someone, but only one partner was formally warned (ishhad) to demolish it. The ruling from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani clarifies that only the warned partner bears liability, and only for his ownership share. Moreover, the payment is not on him personally but on his aqila (agnatic solidarity group). We will unpack the reasoning and the proofs behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Parse the scenario
Identify the five partners, the warning to only one, and the consequence: wall collapse causing death.
2
Identify the ruling
State the hukm: the warned partner is liable for one-fifth of the diyat, and this liability is on his aqila, not personally.
3
Understand the proof
The dalil is qiyas khafi: the obligation to prevent harm (tafriqh) falls only on the warned party, so liability attaches only to him.
The warning (ishhad) is the trigger for liability.
4
Apply the principle of proportionality
Liability is limited to the warned partner's ownership share. Since he owns one-fifth, he owes one-fifth of the diyat.
If ownership were different, the share would adjust accordingly.
5
Recognize the role of the aqila
The diyat is not paid by the individual but by his aqila (male agnates), based on tribal solidarity for accidental killings.
This reflects the Hanafi principle that blood money in non-intentional homicides is on the aqila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دیوار پانچ آدمیوں کی تھی ایک کے پاس اشہاد قائم کیا گیا، انسان اس کے گرنے سے مر گیا تو دیت سب کی عاقلہ پر ہوگی
قال وإذا كان الحائط بين خمسة رجال أشهد على أحدهم فقتل إنسانا ضمن خمس الدية ويكون ذالك على عاقلته
ترجمہ محمد نے فرمایا اور جب کہ دیوار پانچ آدمیوں کے درمیان ہو ان میں سے ایک پر اشہاد کیا گیا ہو پس دیوار نے کسی آدمی کو قتل کر دیا تو وہ (جس پر اشہاد ہوا ہے ) دیت کے خمس کا ضامن ہوگا اور وہ اس کے عاقلہ پر واجب ہوگا۔ تشریح ایک دیوار میں پانچ آدمی شریک ہیں لیکن توڑنے کا مطالبہ صرف ان میں سے ایک ہی سے کیا گیا ہے باقیوں سے نہیں اب کوئی اس دیوار کے گرنے سے ہلاک ہو گیا تو باقی چار پر کچھ ضمان نہ ہوگا کیونکہ ان کے حق میں اشہار نہیں ہے۔ بلکہ اس ایک پر منمان ہو گا اس پر اشہاد ہوا ہے لیکن وہ صرف دیت کے خمس کا ضامن ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس کی ملکیت اسی حساب سے ہے۔ اور یہ دیت کا ۱/۵ بھی اس پر واجب نہ ہوگا بلکہ اس کی برادری پر واجب ہوگا۔
The wall belonged to five men; warning was given to one only. When the wall collapsed and killed a person, the diyat would be upon the aqila of that one. He (Muhammad) said: When a wall is between five men, and warning is given to one of them, and it kills a person, then he is liable for one-fifth of the diyat, and that is upon his aqila. Translation: Muhammad said: When a wall is between five men, and warning is given to one of them, then the wall killed a person, he (the one warned) is liable for one-fifth of the diyat, and that is upon his aqila. Elaboration: Five men share a wall, but only one of them is asked to demolish it. Then someone dies from the wall's collapse. The other four are not liable because they were not warned. Only the warned one is liable, but only for one-fifth of the diyat, because his ownership is one-fifth. And this one-fifth is not upon him personally but upon his 'aqila (agnatic relatives).
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a wall is jointly owned by five people. Only one of them is formally warned (ishhad) about the danger. When the wall kills someone, the liability is only on the warned partner, and only to the extent of his ownership share (one-fifth). The remaining four partners are not liable because they were not given warning. Furthermore, the blood money (diyat) is not paid by the individual but by his 'aqila (male agnates). This ruling is attributed to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani (the author of the Hedaya). It illustrates the Hanafi principle that liability for damages caused by a shared dangerous structure attaches only to those who were specifically warned and only according to their ownership share.

Key Terms
عاقلة (ʿāqila): The male agnates (tribe or clan) who pay blood money on behalf of a killer or liable person.
خمس الدية (khums al-diyah): One-fifth of the full blood money, corresponding to the warned partner's ownership share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If five people jointly own a wall, and only one is warned about its danger, when the wall collapses and kills someone, who pays the diyat?
A) All five partners equally.
B) The warned partner pays the full diyat.
C) The warned partner pays one-fifth of the diyat, and it is paid by his aqila.
D) No one is liable because the warning was insufficient.

Key Takeaway
Only the warned partner is liable for his proportionate share of diyat, paid by his aqila.

Liability for Collapse After Ishhad: Scope of the Warning
Question and answer clarifying scope of ishhad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that ishhad for a dangerous structure covers all foreseeable consequences, including collapse.

Core Idea
The warning (ishhad) for the wall inherently includes its collapse, because the purpose is to prevent obstruction to the path.

In this brief Q&A, a common legal question is raised: if a wall owner is warned only about the wall being dangerous, how can he be liable when it collapses? The answer reveals a key principle of Hanafi liability law — the scope of the warning extends to all foreseeable consequences. Understanding this will help you apply the rule correctly in cases involving dangerous structures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The question challenges the scope of ishhad: does a warning about the wall itself include warning about its collapse?
2
Recognize the purpose of ishhad
The answer states that the purpose is to prevent the property from obstructing the path. The collapse is merely a manifestation of that obstruction.
3
Apply the principle of foreseeability
Since the wall obstructs the path, its collapse is a foreseeable consequence. Thus, the warning covers it.
4
Conclude liability
Because the owner did not remove the obstruction after ishhad, he is liable (daman) for the harm caused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سوال . اشہاد تو فقط دیوار پر تھا نہ کہ ٹوٹن پر تو پھر ضمان کیسا؟ جواب ٹوٹن پر اشہاد ہی ٹوٹن پر اشہاد ہے، اس لئے کہ مقصود تو یہ ہے کہ اس کی ملک راستہ کو نہ گھیرے۔ اور یہاں اس کی ملک نے راستہ کو گھیر رکھا ہے۔
Question: The ishhad was only about the wall, not about its collapse, so how is there liability? Answer: The ishhad for collapse is the same as the ishhad for collapse because the purpose is that his property should not obstruct the path. And here his property is obstructing the path.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a question and answer regarding the scope of ishhad (legal warning). The questioner raises a point: if the authorities only warned the owner about the wall being dangerous, but did not explicitly warn about the possibility of it collapsing and killing someone, how can the owner be held liable? The answer explains that the warning for the wall inherently includes the warning about its collapse. The reason is that the purpose of the ishhad is to ensure the property does not obstruct a public pathway. Since the wall is already an obstruction, its collapse is a foreseeable consequence. Therefore, liability is established. This principle is consistent with the Hanafi view that the cause of liability is the failure to remove the danger after being warned.

Key Terms
اشہاد (ishhad): The legal notification or warning given to the owner of a dangerous structure to remove it, under penalty of liability for any harm caused.
ضمان (daman): Liability, specifically financial compensation (diyah) for causing death.
ملک (milk): Property, ownership. Here refers to the wall that is the property of the owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if a wall owner is warned (ashhad) about the wall being dangerous, and the wall later collapses and kills someone, is the owner liable?
A) Yes, because the warning covers the collapse.
B) No, because the warning was only about the wall, not the collapse.
C) Only if the collapse was specifically predicted.
D) It depends on whether the wall was leaning.

Key Takeaway
Ishhad for a dangerous structure covers all foreseeable consequences, including collapse, because the purpose is to prevent harm from the obstruction.

Liability for Object on Dangerous Wall: The Principle of Tafrig
Ruling on liability for falling object on dangerous wall.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of tafrig to determine who is liable when a death is caused by an object falling from a collapsing structure.

Core Idea
Liability for falling objects follows ownership, not property.

In Hanafi fiqh, when an object placed on a dangerous wall falls and causes death, the liability depends on who owns the object. The key principle is tafrig—the duty to clear the obstruction falls on the owner of the object, not on the owner of the structure. This ruling, from Al-Hidayah, clarifies the distinction and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage first describes a case where the pot belongs to the wall owner, then a case where it belongs to someone else. Note who is liable in each.
The wall owner is already liable for deaths caused directly by the wall itself.
2
State the rule for each scenario
When the pot belongs to the wall owner, he is liable for deaths from the pot. When it belongs to another, that other is liable.
Liability here means paying diyat (blood money).
3
Extract the legal principle: Tafrig
The passage states: 'because the removal (tafrig) is upon him.' Tafrig means clearing the obstruction from the public path. The owner of the object has this duty.
This principle is independent of who owns the wall.
4
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Consider: A person places a flowerpot on a neighbor's leaning wall. The wall collapses, the pot kills a passerby. Who is liable? According to the rule, the flowerpot owner is liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
گرنے والی دیوار پرگھڑ رکھا تھا اور گھڑا بھی مالک مکان کا تھا اس سے کوئی ہلاک ہو گیا مالک دار ضامن ہوگا
ولو عطب بجرة كانت على الحائط فسقطت بسقوطه وهى ملكه ضمنه لأن التفريغ إليه وإن كان ملك غيره لا يضمنه لأن التفريغ إلى مالكها ترجمہ اور اگر وہ ( بکر) اس گھڑے سے پھسلا ہو جو دیوار پر تھا پس دیوار کے گرنے سے وہ گر گیا، حالانکہ وہ گھڑا اس کی (زید کی ) ملکیت ہے تو وہ (زید) اس کا ضامن ہو گا اس لئے کہ تفریغ اس کا کام ہے اور اگر اس کے غیر کی ملک ہو تو ضامن نہ ہو گا اس لئے کہ تفریغ اسکے مالک کی جانب ہے۔ تشریح زید کی جھکی ہوئی دیوار پر مثلاً کوئی شیرہ کا گھڑا رکھا ہے اور دیوار گری جس سے خالد ہلاک ہو گیا تو اس کا ضمان زید کے اوپر ہے۔ اور دیوار کے گرنے سے گھڑا بھی گرا اب اس میں پھسل کر بکر مر گیا تو بکر کا ضمان کس پر ہو گا ؟ تو فرمایا کہ اگر کھڑے کا مالک زید ہی ہے تو منمان زید پر واجب ہوگا اور اگر اس کی دیوار پر کسی اور نے سکھانے کے لئے اپنا گھڑارکھ دیا تو منمان بکر گھڑے کے مالک پر لازم ہوگا۔ وجہ اس کی یہ ہے کہ جو گھڑے کا مالک ہو گا اس کو راستہ میں سے اٹھانا اور راستہ کو صاف کرنا اس کا فریضہ ہے لہذا پہلی صورت میں یہ زید کا فریضہ ہے اور دوسری صورت میں اس کا فریضہ ہے جس کا یہ گھڑا ہے۔
A pot was placed on a falling wall, and the pot also belonged to the owner of the house; someone died from it, so the owner of the house is liable. And if (someone) slips on a pot that was on the wall, and it fell due to the wall's collapse, and it is his property, then he is liable because the removal (tafrig) is upon him. And if it is the property of someone else, he is not liable because the removal is upon its owner. Translation: And if (Bakr) slips on that pot which was on the wall, and due to the wall's collapse it fell, and that pot is his (Zayd's) property, then he (Zayd) is liable for him because removal is his duty. And if it is the property of someone other than him, he is not liable because removal is up to its owner. Explanation: For example, Zayd has a leaning wall, and a pot of syrup is placed on it. The wall falls and kills Khalid, so Zayd is liable. And due to the wall's fall, the pot also falls; then Bakr slips on it and dies. Who is liable for Bakr? It is said: if the pot's owner is Zayd himself, then liability on Zayd. And if someone else placed his own pot on the wall, then liability on the owner of the pot. The reason is that the person who owns the pot has the duty to remove it and clear the path. So in the first case, it is Zayd's duty; in the second, it is the duty of the one who owns the pot.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on liability when a death is caused by an object (a pot) that was on a dangerous wall and falls upon the wall's collapse. The key principle is that liability for the death is based on the duty to remove the object (tafrig). The person who owns the object is responsible for removing it from the path. Therefore, if the pot belongs to the wall owner, he is liable for any death resulting from the pot. If the pot belongs to someone else, that owner is liable, not the wall owner. This is consistent with the Hanafi rule that the one who has the ability to remove the obstruction (tafrig) is liable. The wall owner is already liable for deaths caused directly by the wall itself (as in the case of Khalid dying from the wall), but the pot case is considered separately. The commentary illustrates with a concrete example.

Key Terms
جرة (jarrah): A pot, jar, or large vessel, typically made of clay, used for storing liquids.
تفریغ (tafrig): The act of removing or clearing away an object that obstructs a public pathway. It is the duty of the owner to perform tafrig.
ضمان (daman): Liability for financial compensation in cases of death or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person places his own pot on someone else's dangerous wall, and the wall collapses causing the pot to fall and kill a passerby, who is liable according to this passage?
A) The wall owner.
B) The pot owner.
C) Both equally.
D) Neither, because both were negligent.

Key Takeaway
Liability for death caused by a falling object from a dangerous structure rests with the owner of the object, not the owner of the structure, because the duty to remove the object (tafrig) is on its owner.

Liability for a Shared Wall: Proportional Diyah on the Warned Owner
Ruling on proportion of diyah when a jointly owned wall kills after warning one partner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a jointly owned wall kills after ishhad on only one partner, and identify that only the warned partner pays his proportionate one-fifth share of the diyah through his 'aqlah.

Core Idea
Liability for a collapsing wall follows warning and ownership share: only the warned partner pays his proportional diyah through his 'aqlah.

A wall owned by five partners collapses and kills a person. Only one of the owners had been formally warned (ashhad) to fix it. Who is liable? This passage from Imam Muhammad establishes that liability is conditional upon ishhad and proportional to ownership. The warned partner bears one-fifth of the diyah, and it is paid by his 'aqlah, not by his own wealth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fact pattern
A wall is jointly owned by five individuals. Only one owner has been issued a formal warning (ishhad) about the danger. The wall collapses and kills a person.
Ishhad is a legal notice to remove the hazard; without it, no liability for collapse.
2
Determine who is liable
According to the passage, only the warned partner is liable. The other four bear no liability because they were not warned.
3
Calculate the proportion of diyah
The liable partner owns one-fifth of the wall, so he is liable for one-fifth of the total diyah. Liability is proportional to ownership share.
Full diyah is 100 camels or equivalent; one-fifth = 20 camels or proportionate amount.
4
Identify who pays the diyah
The one-fifth diyah is not paid from the partner's own wealth but is wajib on his 'aqlah (agnatic group of relatives).
In Hanafi law, unintentional killings require diyah on the 'aqlah, not on the individual personally.

دیوار پانچ آدمیوں کی تھی ایک کے پاس اشہاد قائم کیا گیا، انسان اس کے گرنے سے مر گیا تو دیت سب کی عاقلہ پر ہوگی قال وإذا كان الحائط بين خمسة رجال أشهد على أحدهم فقتل إنسانا ضمن خمس الدية ويكون ذالك على عاقلته ترجمہ محمد نے فرمایا اور جب کہ دیوار پانچ آدمیوں کے درمیان ہو ان میں سے ایک پر اشہاد کیا گیا ہو پس دیوار نے کسی آدمی کو قتل کر دیا تو وہ (جس پر اشہاد ہوا ہے ) دیت کے خمس کا ضامن ہوگا اور وہ اس کے عاقلہ پر واجب ہوگا۔ تشریح ایک دیوار میں پانچ آدمی شریک ہیں لیکن توڑنے کا مطالبہ صرف ان میں سے ایک ہی سے کیا گیا ہے باقیوں سے نہیں اب کوئی اس دیوار کے گرنے سے ہلاک ہو گیا تو باقی چار پر کچھ ضمان نہ ہوگا کیونکہ ان کے حق میں اشہار نہیں ہے۔ بلکہ اس ایک پر منمان ہو گا اس پر اشہاد ہوا ہے لیکن وہ صرف دیت کے خمس کا ضامن ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس کی ملکیت اسی حساب سے ہے۔ اور یہ دیت کا ۱/۵ بھی اس پر واجب نہ ہوگا بلکہ اس کی برادری پر واجب ہوگا۔
A wall belonged to five people; ishhad was established against one of them. A person died from its collapse, so the diyah will be on the 'aqlah of all. He (Muhammad) said: And when the wall is between five men, one of them is warned (ashhad), and it kills a person, he is liable for one-fifth of the diyah, and that is on his 'aqlah. Translation: Muhammad said: And when a wall is between five men, and one of them is warned, and the wall kills a person, then he (the warned one) is liable for one-fifth of the diyah, and it is obligatory on his 'aqlah. Explanation: Five partners in a wall, but the demand to break it was made only to one of them, not the others. Now a person dies from its collapse. So there is no liability on the other four because there was no ishhad for them. Rather, liability is on that one, because ishhad was made against him, but he is only liable for one-fifth of the diyah because his ownership is proportional. And this one-fifth of the diyah is not obligatory on him alone, but on his 'aqlah (agnatic group).
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Imam Muhammad that when a wall is owned jointly by five individuals, and only one of them has been properly warned (ashhad) to remove the danger, then if the wall collapses and kills someone, liability is limited to the warned partner. However, he is only liable for his proportionate share of the diyah, which is one-fifth, because his ownership stake is one-fifth. Moreover, this one-fifth diyah is not paid by him personally; rather, it falls on his 'aqlah (the clan or group responsible for blood money) according to Hanafi law. The other four partners bear no liability because they were never warned to remove the danger, and liability only attaches upon ishhad. This is consistent with the Hanafi rule that liability for dangerous structures follows ishhad and ownership proportion.

Key Terms
عاقلہ ('aqlah): The group of agnatic relatives (usually male paternal relatives) who bear the diyah jointly in cases of unintentional killing.
خمس (khums): One-fifth share of the diyah.
دیت (diyah): Blood money, the financial compensation paid to the heirs of a person who is killed, either intentionally or unintentionally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a wall owned by five people collapses and kills someone, but only one of the five was warned (ashhad) to fix it, what is the liability according to this passage?
A) The warned one pays the entire diyah.
B) The warned one pays one-fifth of the diyah, on his 'aqlah.
C) All five pay one-fifth each.
D) No one is liable because the warning was inadequate.

Key Takeaway
Liability for a dangerous structure attaches only to the warned owner, proportionally to his share, and is borne by his 'aqlah.

Liability for Damages on Shared Property Without Consent
Dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the proportion of diyah when one of three partners causes death by digging a well or building a wall without consent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on diyah liability when a partner causes death by digging or building without consent and explain the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Liability for damages on shared property without consent: Abu Hanifa says 2/3, Sahibayn say 1/2.

When a partner builds or digs on shared property without the consent of other partners, causing a death, the Hanafi school has an internal dispute over the proportion of diyah. Imam Abu Hanifa bases liability on the ownership share of the non-consenting partners, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) apply an analogy to cases of multiple independent causes. This lesson unpacks both positions, their proofs, and the practical difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Parties
Three partners share a house. One partner digs a well or builds a wall without consent. Someone dies as a result.
The act is ta'addi (transgression) only regarding the shares of the other partners.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the acting partner is liable for 2/3 of the diyah on his 'aqlah.
Because he transgressed only against the other two partners' shares (2/3), not his own share.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position
The Sahibayn hold that the acting partner is liable for 1/2 of the diyah.
They divide liability into two categories: liability attaches to one part (the warned/transgressed share) and is disregarded for the other.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's Proof
The Sahibayn analogize to an earlier case where a lion wound, snake bite, and man's wound cause death. In that case, the man pays half. Here, similarly, there are two categories (liable and non-liable) so half.
This is qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) because the illa is not directly visible.
5
Compare the Two Reasonings
Abu Hanifa uses ownership ratio; the Sahibayn use a division into two parts based on whether liability is established.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is the amount: 2/3 vs 1/2.

صاحبین کی دلیل
لهما أن التلف بنصيب من أشهد عليه معتبر و بنصيب من لم يشهد عليه هدر فكانا قسمين فانقسم نصفين كما مر في عقر الأسد ونهس الحية وجرح الرجل
ترجمہ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ جس پر اشتہار ہوا ہے اس کے حصہ کے مقابلے میں تلف معتبر ہے اور جس پر اشہاد نہیں ہوا اس کے حصہ کے مقابلہ میں بدر ہے تو یہ دو قسمیں ہو گئیں تو ضمان آدھا آدھا منقسم ہو گیا جیسے گذر گیا شیر کے زخمی کرنے میں اور سانپ کے ڈسنے میں اور آدمی کے زخمی کرنے ہیں ۔
تشریح یہ صاحبین کی دلیل ہے جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ یہاں ایک پر اشتہار ہوا ہے باقیوں پر نہیں ہوا تو اول پر منمان ہوگا اور باقیوں پر ضمان نہ ہو گا۔ اسی طرح جس نے کنواں کھودا، اس نے تعدی کی اور جنہوں نے نہیں کھودا ، انہوں نے کوئی تعدی نہیں کی۔ خلاصہ کلام یہاں ان دونوں مثالوں میں فعل دو ستم کے ہو گئے ایک وہ جس میں تعدی ہے اور دوسرا وہ جس میں تعدی نہیں ہے۔ لہذا انمان بھی دو ہی حصوں پر منقسم ہوگا تو نصف زمان کو ادا کرنا اس متعدی کا فریضہ ہوگا اور باقی بدر ہوگا۔
اس کی مثال بعینہ ایسی ہے کہ زید کو شیر نے پھاڑا اور سانپ نے اس کو ڈسا اور کسی شخص نے اس کو زخمی کیا اور ان تینوں کی وجہ سے وہ مر
Three partners had a house; one dug a well or built a wall without the consent of the other two; someone died from it. How much diyah is obligatory on him? The sayings of the jurists: And if a house is between three people, and one of them digs a well in it, or the digging is without the consent of the other two partners, or builds a wall, and a person is struck by it, then upon him is two-thirds of the diyah on his 'aqlah. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And they (the two) said: upon him is half the diyah on his 'aqlah in both cases. Explanation: Three persons share a house. One, without the consent of the other two, digs a well or builds a wall. Someone dies in the well or due to the wall. Then this builder or digger is liable for two-thirds of the diyah. This is Abu Hanifa's saying. According to the Sahibayn, he is liable for half. It is as if Imam Abu Hanifa says: This partner is a transgressor (zalim) with respect to the shares of his two partners because of his transgression (ta'addi), but he is not a transgressor with respect to his own share. Therefore, there is no liability (daman) on him for his own share. And due to his transgression in his partners' shares, he is liable, and since their share is 2/3, he is liable for 2/3 of the diyah. The Sahibayn's proof: For them, that the damage (talaf) is considered (mu'tabar) with respect to the share of the one against whom warning was given (ashhadu 'alayhi), and it is disregarded (hadar) with respect to the share of one who was not warned. Thus, they become two parts, so they divide into two halves, as previously in the case of a lion's wound, a snake's bite, and a man's wound. Explanation: This is the Sahibayn's proof. Its gist: Here, one was warned, the others were not. So there is liability for the first, and no liability for the others. Similarly, the one who dug the well committed transgression, while those who did not dig did not commit transgression. In both examples, the act became two parts: one with transgression, one without. Therefore, the liability is divided into two halves. So paying half is the duty of the transgressor, and the rest is disregarded. The exact example: Zayd was torn by a lion, bitten by a snake, and wounded by a man, and due to all three, he dies.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liability when one of three partners in a house causes death by digging a well or building a wall without the consent of the other two. The death may occur by falling into the well or by the wall collapsing. The question is: what proportion of the diyah is incumbent upon the acting partner? Imam Abu Hanifa holds that he is liable for two-thirds of the diyah, and it is to be paid by his 'aqlah. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) hold that he is liable for half. Abu Hanifa's reasoning (explained in the commentary) is that the acting partner has transgressed (ta'addi) with respect to the shares of the other two partners (who did not consent) but is not a transgressor regarding his own share (since he owns part of the house). Therefore, he is liable only for the value of the shares of the others, which is two-thirds. The Sahibayn's proof draws an analogy to cases where multiple independent causes lead to death. They argue that the situation involves two categories: the share where liability attaches (due to transgression or warning) and the share where it does not. These are two distinct categories, and liability should be divided equally. They analogize to the case of a lion wound, a snake bite, and a human wound, where if all three contribute to death, the liability is shared? Actually the example: if a lion wounds, a snake bites, and a man wounds, and the person dies, the man is liable for half? The text says 'as previously in the case...' It refers to an earlier ruling where if a lion, snake, and man each contribute equally to a death, the man is liable for half the diyah? The commentary clarifies that the Sahibayn's proof is that the harm is considered with respect to the warned share and disregarded for the unwarned share, so liability is half. The exact ruling of that earlier case is not fully elaborated here, but the point is the analogy. This is a key intra-Hanafi difference with practical consequence: in the absence of consent, the acting partner's liability is either 2/3 (according to Abu Hanifa) or 1/2 (according to the Sahibayn). The text does not specify which view is mufta bihi, but later Hanafi works may prefer the Sahibayn's view or Abu Hanifa's. The commentary presents both views and explains the reasoning.

Key Terms
تعدی (ta'addi): Transgression; exceeding one's right or acting without consent. In this context, digging a well or building a wall on shared property without the partners' consent.
صاحبین (Sahibayn): Abu Yusuf (Ya'qub bin Ibrahim) and Muhammad bin Hasan al-Shaybani, the two principal disciples of Imam Abu Hanifa.
عاقلہ ('aqlah): The agnatic group responsible for paying diyah on behalf of a liable person.
دیت (diyah): Blood money; full diyah for a life is 100 camels or equivalent.
نصفین (nisfayn): Two halves; half and half.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Regarding the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the liability of a partner who digs a well without consent, what is the Sahibayn's proof based on?
A) The partner acted alone.
B) Analogy to a case where a lion, snake, and man cause death.
C) The consent of the other partners was irrelevant.
D) The partner owned the entire house.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when a partner causes death on shared property without consent, liability is not full but proportional, with Imam Abu Hanifa favoring two-thirds and the Sahibayn one-half.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
15 min
Liability of a Partner Who Digs a Well or Builds a Wall Without Consent
Intra-Hanafi dispute over diyat liability share for a partner causing death on shared property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Imam Abu Hanifah's and the Sahibayn's rulings on diyat liability for a partner acting without consent on shared property, and evaluate their respective proofs.

Core Idea
A partner's liability for causing death on shared property depends on whether the cause is unitary or divisible: Abu Hanifah apportions by ownership, the Sahibayn by transgression division.

The Hanafi school, like all madhahib, contains internal disagreements among its founding imams. This passage presents one such dispute: a partner digs a well or builds a wall on jointly owned property without the consent of the other partners, and someone dies as a result. How much blood money (diyat) is the acting partner liable for? Imam Abu Hanifah and his two foremost disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), differ on the answer. Their disagreement stems from different conceptions of the cause of death and how to apportion liability. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze tort liability in Hanafi fiqh and appreciate the reasoning behind differing opinions.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifah)
Identify that Imam Abu Hanifah rules that the partner who acted without consent is liable for two-thirds of the diyat (if there are three partners total). Recognize that this is based on dividing liability according to ownership shares: the partner is a wrongdoer only in the shares of the other two (2/3), not in his own share.
The property is divided into thirds; the actor's own third is not subject to liability.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's opposition
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the partner is liable for only half of the diyat. They consider the act as divisible into two categories: the part with transgression (the shares of the non-consenting partners) and the part without (the actor's own share).
They treat the whole act as giving rise to two equal portions, hence half liability.
3
Follow the Sahibayn's proof (qiyas to multiple tortfeasors)
The Sahibayn analogize to a case where a lion, a snake, and a man each wound a person, and the wounds together cause death. Only half the diyat is imposed on the man because the lion and snake are not liable. Similarly, here the partner's own share is like the non-liable tortfeasors, so only half is attributed to transgression.
Their qiyas relies on the idea that the cause of death is divisible into independent contributions.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifah's response (unity of cause)
Abu Hanifah rejects the analogy. He argues that death is caused by a single cause: the specific weight or depth that makes the well or wall lethal. This single cause cannot be split into independent parts; therefore, liability must be divided according to ownership of the property, not by a presumed division of the act. So the actor is liable for the shares of the other partners only.
The core disagreement is about the nature of the cause: unitary versus divisible.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is the amount of diyat: Abu Hanifah says two-thirds, the Sahibayn say half. Both positions are recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah and are considered valid within the Hanafi school, though later practice may prefer one view.
No explicit tarjih is provided in this passage.
