And if one of them is free and the other a slave, then in accidental, the value of the slave is wajib upon the 'aqila of the free man who is killed. His heirs take it, and the right of the free man slain in diyah beyond that value becomes void. Because according to the principle of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the value is wajib on the 'aqila because it is a guarantee of a person; he has left a substitute to this extent, so the heirs of the free man take it, and what exceeds it is void due to lack of substitute. Translation: And if one of them is free and the other a slave, then in accidental, the value of the slave is wajib upon the 'aqila of the slain free man. So the heirs of the free man take it, and the right of the free man in diyah beyond that value becomes void. Because according to the principle of both sides, the value is wajib on the 'aqila because it is a guarantee of a person; the slave left a substitute to that extent, so the heirs of the free man take it, and what exceeds his right is void due to lack of substitute. Commentary: Zayd and Khalid accidentally collided and both died. Zayd is the slave and Khalid is free. In this case, the 'aqila (tribal group) of Khalid will pay the full value of Zayd, and that value will be taken by the heirs of Khalid. However, the diyah of Khalid, because he is free, is larger. But since Zayd left only the value as a substitute, the heirs of Khalid will take only that, and the remaining amount of the diyah will be declared void and worthless due to lack of substitute. And the value received from the slave is not as property but as a guarantee of a person, and the diyah of a person is on the 'aqila, therefore according to both sides (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), this value is wajib upon Khalid's tribe.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage details the ruling when a free man and a slave accidentally collide and both die. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the 'aqila (responsible group) of the free man must pay the market value of the slave to the free man's heirs. The slave's value becomes the substitute (khalaf) for the free man's diyah, which is much larger. Since the slave left no other substitute, the free man's diyah is limited to the slave's value; any excess over that value is void (hader). The commentary illustrates with an example: Zayd (slave) and Khalid (free) die in accident. The 'aqila of Khalid pays Zayd's full value to Khalid's heirs. Although Khalid's diyah as a free man is higher, only the value is paid because the slave's estate cannot cover more. The value is considered a guarantee of a person (daman al-adami), not mere property, so it is wajib on the 'aqila.

Key Terms
عاقلہ ('aqila): The tribal group or relatives responsible for paying diyah on behalf of a perpetrator.
خلیفہ (khalaf): Substitute or replacement; in this context, the value of the slave that stands in for the free man's diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The full diyah of the free man
B) The full diyah of the slave
C) The value of the slave
D) Half the value of the slave

Key Takeaway
In an accidental collision between a free man and a slave, the free man's 'aqila pays the slave's value as a substitute; the free man's own diyah is limited to that amount.

Diyat — Blood Money

Rule When Two Slaves Collide and Both Die
Statement of a ruling of blood wasting (hadar) for colliding slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two slaves die by collision and explain why no compensation is due.

Core Idea
When both slaves die, compensation is impossible, so their blood is wasted.

This lesson presents a specific ruling from the Book of Diyat: if two slaves collide and both die, whether intentionally or accidentally, their blood is 'hadar' (wasted) and no financial compensation is owed. The reasoning hinges on the procedural impossibility of the standard options available when a slave commits homicide. Understanding this ruling illuminates how Hanafi fiqh treats slaves as property with limited agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and outcome
Note that both colliding persons are slaves (ghulam) and both die as a result of the collision. The passage covers both intentional and accidental scenarios.
2
State the ruling
The ruling is that the blood of both slaves is hadar (wasted) and no compensation (daman) is due.
3
Understand the standard options for a slave's crime
Hanafi law gives two options when a slave commits a homicide: the slave is handed over to the victim's heirs, or the master pays a ransom (fidyah) to keep the slave.
4
Apply the impossibility analysis
Both options fail here: the slave cannot be handed over because he is dead, and there is no property left to pay a ransom. Therefore, compensation is impossible.
The slave left no khalaf (wealth) – so the master has nothing to pay.
5
Conclude the ruling
Since neither option is possible, the only remaining outcome is that the blood is wasted (hadar). This is a pure hukm wad'i (declaratory ruling).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات
۲۳۸
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۵ تشریح اور اگر ٹکرانے والے دونوں غلام ہوں عمد انکر ہو یا خطاء بہر دو صورت دونوں غلاموں کا خون ہدر ہوگا اور کوئی ضمان نہیں آئے گا۔ کیونکہ جب غلام جنایت کرتا ہے تو اس کی دو ہی صورتیں ہوتی ہیں۔ یا تو وہی غلام مجنی علیہ کے اولیاء کے سپر د کیا جاتا ہے یا آقا غلام کو اپنے پاس روک لیتا ہے اور اس کا فدیہ ادا کر دیتا ہے اور یہاں کوئی صورت ممکن نہیں ہے۔ اول تو اس لئے کہ غلام مر گیا تو کیا سپرد کیا جائے اور ثانی اس لئے کہ جب غلام نے اپنا کچھ خلیفہ نہیں چھوڑا تو آقا ء کس کا فدیہ ادا کرے۔ لہذا ہدر کے علاوہ کوئی چارہ کار نہ ملا۔
Book of Blood Money. Page 238. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary on Hidayah, volume 15. Explanation: If both colliding persons are slaves, whether intentional or accidental, in both cases the blood of both slaves is wasted and no compensation is owed. Because when a slave commits a crime, there are only two possibilities: either the slave himself is handed over to the heirs of the victim, or the master keeps the slave and pays a ransom. Here neither is possible: first, because the slave died, so what can be handed over? Second, because the slave left no property (khalaf), so what ransom would the master pay? Hence there is no option except that the blood is wasted.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage states a ruling about two slaves colliding and both dying. The ruling is that regardless of whether the killing was intentional or accidental, the blood of both slaves is hadar (wasted), meaning no blood money or compensation is owed. The reason given is procedural: in cases of a slave's crime, the options are either to hand over the slave to the victim's heirs (for punishment or enslavement) or for the master to pay a ransom (fidyah) and keep the slave. Here both options are impossible because the slave is dead (cannot be handed over) and has left no wealth (khalaf) for the master to pay. Therefore, the only outcome is that the blood goes uncompensated. This ruling is specific to slaves and does not apply to free persons.

Key Terms
هدر (hadar): Blood that is wasted (no compensation or qisas).
غلام (ghulam): A male slave (categorized as property with legal capacity).
ضمان (daman): Liability or compensation (for injury or death).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The master of each slave must pay half the value of the other slave.
B) The blood of both slaves is wasted (hadar) and no compensation is due.
C) The masters must pay the full value of the slaves to the victim's families.
D) The slaves' blood money is reduced by half.

Key Takeaway
When two slaves die by collision, their blood is hadar (wasted) because no compensation mechanism is possible.

One Free and One Slave Collide Intentionally
Determines half the slave's value due from free's aqila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a free man and a slave both die in an intentional collision, and explain why the free man's 'aqila pays only half the slave's value.

Core Idea
In intentional killing, compensation is halved; the slave's liability falls except for the substitute he left.

This lesson examines a specific case from diyat (blood money) where two persons—one free, one slave—both die in an intentional collision. The Hanafi school provides a unique ruling that balances the principles of 'amd (intentional killing) and the slave's limited capacity. We will unpack the reasoning behind why only half the slave's value is due.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
Two persons collide intentionally, both die. One is free, the other a slave. Ruling: free man's 'aqila pays half the market value of the slave to the slave's heirs.
2
Understand the rationale of 'amd
In intentional killing, the diyah is only half because the killer may also face qisas. This general principle (al-madmun huwa al-nisf fi al-'amd) is applied here.
This is the key legal principle that drives the halving.
3
Consider the slave's liability
The slave owed half the free man's diyah on his neck (because he killed a free man intentionally). But since he died, that obligation falls except for the substitute he left behind, which is half his own value.
The 'badal' (substitute) is part of the slave's estate.
4
Determine the practical outcome
The free man's heirs receive half the slave's value from the free man's 'aqila. The remaining half of the slave's value (that was due from him) is waived due to his death.
So net compensation is exactly half the slave's value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفى العمد تجب على عاقلة الحر نصف قيمة العبد لأن المضمون هو النصف فى العمد وهذا القدر يأخذه ولى المقتول وما على العبد في رقبته وهو نصف دية الحر يسقط بموته إلا قدر ما أخلف من البدل و هو نصف القيمة.

تشریح عمد کی صورت میں چونکہ نصف دیت واجب ہوا کرتی ہے ( کمامز )۔ لہذا اگر ایک غلام اور ایک آزاد ہونے کی صورت میں خطاء کے بجائے عمد انکر ہوئی تو کیا حکم ہے؟ تو فرمایا کہ خالد کی مددگار برادری پر زید کی آدھی قیمت واجب ہوگی اور اس نصف قیمت کو خالد کے در شالیں گے۔ سوال یہاں تو غلام پر ( زید پر ) خالد کی (آزاد کی ) آدھی قیمت واجب ہونی چاہیئے ؟ جواب مگر چونکہ زید نے کوئی خلیفہ نہیں چھوڑا علاوہ اس مقدار کے یعنی نصف قیمت کے تو بس اسی قدر مقتول خر ( خالد ) کے ورثہ کو ملے گا اور باقی مقدار ساقط ہو گی ۔
In intentional killing, half the value of the slave is owed on the 'aqila of the free victim, because what is compensated in 'amd is half; the guardian of the victim takes this amount. The slave owed half the diyah of a free man on his neck, which falls by his death except for the amount he left as substitute, which is half the value. Translation and explanation follow.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling when a free man and a slave collide intentionally (both die). The ruling: the 'aqila of the free victim must pay half the market value of the slave. The reason is that in intentional killing, the diyah is normally only half of the full diyah (since the killer may face qisas). Here, the slave owed half of a free man's diyah on his neck (i.e., if he killed a free man intentionally, his liability is half), but since the slave died, that obligation falls except for the substitute he left, which is half his value. So the free victim's heirs receive half the slave's value, and the rest is lost.

Key Terms
عمد ('amd): Intentional killing; in Hanafi law, diyah is half of full diyah (the other half is qisas) if the killer is not executed.
نصف قيمة (nisaf qimah): Half the market value of the slave; the amount owed due to the limitation of compensation in 'amd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Half the value of the slave.
C) The full diyah of a free man.
D) Nothing, because the free man is also a victim.

Key Takeaway
In intentional collision between free and slave, the free man's 'aqila pays half the slave's value because compensation in intentional killing is halved.

Liability of Driver for Falling Equipment from Animal
Ruling with reasoning on liability for falling load from driven animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when equipment falls from a driven animal and kills someone, and identify the key factor of negligence (taqsir) that triggers liability.

Core Idea
The driver is liable only if his negligence caused the fall – mere accident is not enough.

In Hanafi tort law, the driver of an animal is responsible for the safety of the equipment and load he carries. This passage from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani establishes the general rule: if the saddle, bridle, or load falls and kills someone, the driver is liable. However, this liability is not absolute – it depends on a finding of negligence. Understanding the conditions and exceptions is essential for applying this rule correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act: driving an animal; the equipment falls and kills a person. Ruling: The driver is liable (daman).
2
Determine the basis of liability
The driver is considered muta'addi (transgressor) because his inadequate securing of the equipment (taqsir) caused the harm.
3
Analyse the exception
Items like loose clothing are not customarily secured; thus no liability because no negligence.
4
Note the conditional nature
The ruling is conditional upon safety; if the driver secures everything properly and something still falls, he may not be liable.
5
Apply to new cases
Use the principle of negligence to determine liability in analogous situations, such as falling loads from vehicles.

ال ومن ساق دابة فوقع السرج على رجل فقتله ضمن وكذا على هذا سائر دواته كاللجام ونحوه وكذا ما حمل عليها لأنه متعد في هذا التسبيب لأن الوقوع بتقصير منه وهو ترك الشد والاحكام فيه بخلاف الرداء لأنه لايشد فى العادة ولأنه قاصد لحفظ هذه الأشياء كما في المحمول على عاتقه دون اللباس على ما مر من قبل فيتقيد بشرط السلامة.

تشریح زید چوپایہ کو ہانکے جارہا ہے اس کی زمین با لگام یا وہ سامان جو اس کے اوپر لدا ہوا ہے کچھ گر گیا جس سے کوئی شخص ہلاک ہو گیا ان تمام صورتوں میں زید پر منمان ہو گا۔ اس لئے کہ یہاں زید قتل کا ہے اور بے اندر تعدی ہوتی ہے تو اس پر ضمان واجب ہوتا ہے لہذا یہاں مان واجب ہوگا۔

کیونکہ یہاں چادر کے نہ باندھنے میں کوئی تعدی نہیں ہے کیونکہ چادر عموم و عادہ باندھی نہیں جاتی ۔
پھر لباس کا مقصد لباس کی حفاظت نہیں ہوتا اور ان سامان کو لادنے اور لے جانے کا مقصد انکی حفاظت ہے، لہذا ان کو لے جانا سلامتی ما شرط سے مقید ہوگا اور اگر سلامتی معدوم ہو جائے تو ضمان واجب ہوگا۔
Muhammad (al-Shaybani) said: Whoever drives an animal and the saddle falls on a person and kills him, he is liable. Similarly for other equipment like the bridle and the like, and also for what is loaded on it, because he is transgressing (muta'addi) in this causation. Because the fall is due to his negligence in not tying and securing it properly. Unlike a (loose) garment because it is not customarily tied. And because he intends to preserve these things, unlike clothing. The ruling is conditional upon safety.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani (the author of al-Hidayah's original matn) establishes liability for a person driving an animal when something from the animal's equipment or load falls and kills someone. The driver is liable (damin) because he is considered muta'addi (transgressor) in causing the death. The transgression is his negligence in failing to properly tie or secure the saddle, bridle, or load. The exception is a garment like a cloak (ridaa') that is not customarily tied; if it falls and kills, there is no liability because there is no negligence. The driver's purpose is to preserve the equipment/load, unlike clothing which is just worn. Therefore, the ruling is tied to a condition of safety: if he secures everything properly and still something goes wrong, he may not be liable (taqayyud bi-shart al-salamah).

Key Terms
سائق (sa'iq): The person driving an animal (typically from behind).
متعدي (muta'addi): Transgressor; one who acts beyond what is lawful or fails to fulfill a duty of care.
تسبيب (tasbib): Causation; the act of causing harm indirectly through an action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to Imam Muhammad, if a driver's saddle falls from an animal and kills someone, is he liable?
A) No, because the saddle falling is an accident.
B) Yes, because he is liable for all equipment he uses.
C) Yes, because he failed to secure the saddle, which is negligence.
D) No, unless he intended to kill.

Key Takeaway
The driver's liability for a killing fall is conditional upon his negligence in securing the animal's equipment or load.

Liability of the Leader (Qa'id) in a Camel Caravan
Ruling on the leader's liability when his camel tramples someone

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the liability of a caravan leader when a camel tramples someone, explain the rationale (tasbib with transgression), and apply the ruling to distinguish between liability for human life and property.

Core Idea
A caravan leader is liable for harm caused by his negligence in protection, even if he did not directly cause it.

Imagine a caravan of camels moving in a line, with a person holding the rein of the front camel. If any camel in that line tramples a person or damages property, who is responsible? The Hanafi school assigns liability to the leader (qa'id) based on his duty to protect the caravan and his negligence if harm occurs. This ruling illustrates the important Hanafi principle that indirect causation (tasbib) only creates liability when accompanied by transgression (ta'addi).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the liable party
The act is a camel trampling a human or property. The liable party is the leader (qa'id), the person who leads the caravan by the front camel's rein.
Even if the trampling camel is not the one the leader holds, he is still liable because he is responsible for the entire caravan.
2
Understand the proof: why the leader is liable
The proof given is that the leader has the duty (it is wajib upon him) to protect the caravan, and he is able to do so. By failing to protect (negligence), he becomes a transgressor (mut'addi). The general principle is that causation (tasbib) with the attribute of transgression (ta'addi) is a cause of liability (daman).
This is a rational proof (talil) based on duty and ability, not direct textual evidence.
3
Distinguish between liability for human life and property
If a camel tramples a human, the diyah (blood money) is on the leader's 'aqila (tribal group). If property is trampled, the compensation is on the leader's own wealth.
The 'aqila is the group of male agnate relatives who typically pay diyah for unintentional killings in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Recognize the key principle: tasbib with ta'addi
The leader is a cause (sabab) but not the direct cause (mubasharah). Hanafi law holds that a cause only incurs liability when the cause involves transgression (i.e., negligence or wrongdoing). Here, the leader's negligence makes the causation 'with ta'addi', so he is liable.
This principle limits liability for indirect causation to cases where the person had a duty to prevent harm and failed.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference (optional)
Other schools may require direct causation for liability, while the Hanafi school considers indirect causation with negligence sufficient. This is a point of ikhtilaf.
Understanding this difference helps appreciate the Hanafi emphasis on duty and responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۵
قطار میں چلتے ہوئے اونٹوں نے اگر کسی کو ہلاک کر دیا تو ضمان کس پر ہوگا ؟
ومن قاد قطارا فهو ضامن لما أوطأ فإن وطىء بعير إنسانا ضمن به الدية على العاقلة لأن القائد عليه حفظ القطار كالسائق وقد أمكنه ذالك وقد صار متعديا بالتقصير فيه والتسبيب بوصف التعدى سبب الضمان إلا أن ضمان النفس على العاقلة فيه وضمان المال فى ماله .
ترجمہ اور جس نے اونٹوں کی قطار کھینچی تو وہ ضامن ہے اس چیز کا جس کو اس نے روندا پس اگر اونٹ نے کسی انسان کو روندا تو اس کے عاقلہ پر دیت کا ضمان ہو گا اس لئے کہ قائد اس کے اوپر قطار کی حفاظت ضروری ہے سائق نے مثل اور قائد کو یہ حفاظت ممکن ہے اور قائد اس میں کوتا ہی کرنے کی وجہ سے متعدی ہو گیا اور تسبیب تعدی کے وصف کے ساتھ ضمان کا سبب ہے مگر نفس کا ضمان تسبیب میں عاقلہ پر ہوتا ہے اور مال کا ضمان مسبب کے مال میں ۔ تشریح اونٹوں کی قطار چل رہی ہے اور ایک شخص اگلے اونٹ کی نکیل پکڑ کر آگے آگے چل رہا ہے باقی سب اس کے پیچھے چل رہے ہیں تو اس کو قائد کہتے ہیں اور اونٹ بلا تکلف اس طرح چلتے ہیں تو مذکورہ صورت میں اگر کسی اونٹ نے کسی انسان کو ہلاک کر دیا تو ضمان واجب ہوگا، یعنی اگر نفس ہے تو اس کا منمان قائد کے عاقلہ پر ہوگا۔ اور اگر مال ہے تو اس کا ضمان خود قائد کے مال میں ہوگا۔ اور یہاں ضمان واجب ہونے کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ قائد پر قطار کی حفاظت ضروری ہے اور یہ حفاظت کر بھی سکتا ہے ویسے تو یہ مسبب ہے سبب پر بصورت تعدی ضمان واجب ہوتا ہے اور یہاں قطار کی حفاظت کو چھوڑ دینا اس کی جانب سے تعدی ہے۔
Book of Blood Money. Ashraf al-Hidaya Urdu Commentary of Hidaya Volume 15. If camels walking in a line kill someone, who is liable? And whoever leads a caravan is liable for what he tramples. If a camel tramples a human, he is liable for the blood money on the 'aqila, because the leader must guard the caravan like the driver, and he is able to do so, and he becomes a transgressor by his negligence, and causation with the attribute of transgression is a cause of liability, except that liability for a human is on the 'aqila and liability for property is on his own wealth. Explanation: A caravan of camels is moving, and a person holds the rein of the front camel and walks ahead; the rest follow behind. He is called the leader. In the mentioned case, if a camel kills a human, liability becomes obligatory: if it is a human, the blood money is on the leader's 'aqila; if it is property, its liability is on the leader's own wealth. The reason liability is obligatory is that the leader must protect the caravan and can do so. He is a cause; liability is only obligatorily due on a cause with transgression. Here, leaving the protection is transgression from his side.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the liability of the leader (qa'id) of a camel caravan. The Arabic matn from the Hidayah states that if someone leads a caravan (qitar) and a camel tramples a person, the leader is liable for the blood money (diyah) on his 'aqila (tribal responsibility group) for a human, and on his own wealth for property. The proof given is that the leader has the duty and ability to protect the caravan; by neglecting this, he becomes a transgressor (mut'addi). Causation (tasbib) with the attribute of transgression is a basis for liability. The commentary then explains the scenario: the leader holds the front camel's rein; if any camel in the line tramples someone, the leader is liable because his negligence caused the harm. This is a clear application of the Hanafi principle that a cause (sabab) only incurs liability when accompanied by transgression.

Key Terms
قائد (qa'id): The person who leads the caravan by holding the rein of the front camel.
عاقلہ ('aqila): The group of male agnate relatives (asabah) of the liable person who pay blood money collectively.
دیت (diyah): Blood money, a fixed financial compensation paid to the heirs of a homicide victim.
تسبیب (tasbib): Indirect causation, where the act is not the direct cause but sets in motion a chain leading to harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The leader holding the rein of the camel that actually trampled
B) The leader's negligence in protecting the caravan
C) The leader owning all the camels in the caravan
D) The camel being wild and uncontrollable

Key Takeaway
A caravan leader is liable for harm caused by any camel in his caravan due to his negligence in protection, with diyah on his 'aqila for human life and on his own wealth for property.

Joint Liability of Leader and Driver (Qa'id and Sa'iq)
Ruling on joint liability of leader and driver

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on joint liability of a leader and driver, and apply the exception when the driver is positioned in the middle of the caravan.

Core Idea
Liability follows effective control: both leader and driver are jointly liable unless the driver's position divides control.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario from the law of diyat: when both a leader (qa'id) and a driver (sa'iq) are present in a camel caravan, who is liable for harm caused by the animals? The Hanafi school holds that liability depends on the connection of reins and the position of the driver. By the end, you will understand how the school allocates liability based on effective control, and the important exception when the driver is in the middle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their roles
Understand the distinction between a qa'id (leader who walks ahead, pulling the reins) and a sa'iq (driver who drives from behind or side). In the basic case, both are present.
The key is that both can have control over the camels.
2
State the default ruling when reins are connected
When the driver is at the back and all reins are linked (connected), both the leader and driver are fully liable for any harm caused by any camel in the caravan. Reason: each has control over the collective due to the connected reins.
This is the general rule.
3
Identify the exception when reins are disconnected
If the driver moves to the middle and takes hold of only one rein, the reins become disconnected. Then liability splits: the driver alone is liable for camels behind him (he becomes their de facto leader), while both are liable for camels in front of the driver.
The original leader retains liability for the front segment.
4
Apply the ruling to a concrete scenario
Practice by imagining Zayd (leader) and Khalid (driver). If Khalid is at the back, both liable for any incident. If Khalid is in the middle, he alone liable for harm from behind, both liable for harm ahead.
The operative principle: liability follows the effective control over the specific animal.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some other schools might hold only the direct actor (the one who held the rein at the moment) liable. The Hanafi position is based on extended causation and the unity of control through connected reins.
This is a point of valid ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قائد اور سائق دونوں پر ضمان ہوگا
وإن كان معه سائق فالضمان عليهما لأن قائد الواحد قائد للكل وكذا سائقه لاتصال الأزمة وهذا إذا كان السائق في جانب من الإبل أما إذا كان توسطها وأخذ بزمام واحد ما يضمن ما عطب بما هو خلفه ويضمنان ما تلف ما بين يديه لأن القائد لا يقود ما خلف السائق لانفصام الزمام والسائق يسوق ما يكون قدامه
ترجمہ اور اگر قائد کے ساتھ کوئی سائق ہو تو ضمان ان دونوں پر ہو گا اس لئے کہ ایک اونٹ کا قائد تمام کا قائد ہے اور ایسے ہی ایک کا سائق ( تمام کا سائق ہے ) نکیلوں کے متصل ہونے کی وجہ سے اور یہ جب ہے جب کہ سائق اونٹوں کی جانب میں ہو، بہر حال سائق جب کہ قطار کے درمیان میں ہو اور ایک کی تکمیل پکڑ لی ہو تو وہ ضامن ہوگا اس کا جو ہلاک ہوا اس اونٹ سے جو اس کے پیچھے ہے اور یہ دونوں ( قائد و سائق ) ضامن ہوں گے اس کے جو سائق کے آگے ہلاک ہوا ہے اس لئے کہ قائدہ ان اونٹوں کا قائد نہیں ہے جو سائق کے یچھے ہیں ۔ تکمیل کے جدا ہونے کی وجہ سے اور سائق ہانکتا ہے ان اونٹوں کو جو اس کے آگے ہیں۔ تشریح زید قائد ہے جس پر پہلی صورت میں ضمان واجب ہوا تھا مگر اس کے ساتھ قائد بھی ہے جو سائق ہے تو اب منمان دونوں پر آئے گا، کیونکہ ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک قائد بھی ہے اور سائق بھی مگر یہ حکم اس وقت ہے جب کہ خالد اونوں کے بالکل پیچھے ہو۔ اور اگر خالد نے قطار کے بیچ میں ہو کر ایک اونٹ کی مہار پکڑ لی ہو تو اب زید سب کا قائد نہیں رہا بلکہ خالد جہاں ہے یہاں تک کا قائد ہے اور خالد اپنے سے اگلوں کا سائق اور اپنے سے پچھلوں کا قائد ہے لہذا ایسی صورت میں جب کہ مہاروں کا اتصال ختم ہو چکا ہے اگر
خالد سے آگے کوئی اونٹ کسی کو ہلاک کر دے تو زید اور خالد دونوں پر ضمان آئے گا۔ اور اگر خالد کے پیچھے یہ واقعہ پیش آئے تو اس کا ضامن فقط خالد ہوگا ، زید نہ ہوگا۔
Both the leader and driver will be liable. And if there is a driver with him, then liability is on both, because the leader of one is the leader of all, and similarly its driver, due to the connection of reins. This is when the driver is on the side of the camels. However, if he is in the middle and takes hold of one rein, then he is liable for what is destroyed from what is behind him, and both are liable for what is destroyed from what is in front of him, because the leader does not lead what is behind the driver due to the reins being separated, and the driver drives what is in front of him. Explanation: Zayd is the leader who was liable in the first case, but now there is also a driver; so liability is on both, because each of them is both leader and driver. But this is when Khalid (the driver) is at the very back of the camels. If Khalid is in the middle and takes hold of one rein, then Zayd is no longer the leader of all; rather, Khalid is the leader up to where he is, and Khalid is the driver of what is in front of him and the leader of what is behind him. So in such a case, when the reins are disconnected, if a camel ahead of Khalid kills someone, both Zayd and Khalid are liable. But if it happens behind Khalid, only Khalid is liable, not Zayd.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage expands on the previous ruling by analyzing the scenario where both a leader (qa'id) and a driver (sa'iq) are present in a camel caravan. The Arabic matn states that if a driver accompanies the leader, both are liable for any harm caused by the caravan because the reins are connected, making each responsible for the whole. However, there is an exception: if the driver is positioned in the middle of the caravan and takes hold of only one camel's rein, then the liability is divided. The driver alone is liable for what happens behind him (because he is the effective leader for those camels), and both leader and driver are jointly liable for what happens in front of the driver (because the original leader is still responsible for the front part). The Urdu commentary clarifies using the names Zayd for the original leader and Khalid for the driver. It explains that if the driver is at the back, both are fully liable; if the driver is in the middle, the driver becomes the leader for the back segment and the driver for the front segment, resulting in the division of liability. This demonstrates the Hanafi principle that liability follows effective control and causation.

Key Terms
سائق (sa'iq): The driver of the caravan, who drives from behind or the side; in this context, a person who also has control over some camels.
زمام (zimam): Rein or halter used to lead camels; plural: azimma. Connection of reins implies unity of control.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for loss or injury, usually in terms of money or blood money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a driver (sa'iq) is positioned in the middle of a camel caravan and takes hold of only one rein, and a camel behind him tramples a person, who is liable?
A) The leader (qa'id) only
B) The driver (sa'iq) only
C) Both leader and driver
D) Neither, since the driver did not directly cause it

Key Takeaway
Liability for a caravan's harm follows effective control: joint if reins are connected, split if the driver's position separates them.

Liability When a Stranger Ties His Camel to the Caravan Without the Leader's Knowledge
Ruling on the leader's liability for a stranger's camel tied to the caravan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for liability when a stranger's camel tied to a caravan causes a death, and explain the leader's recourse against the stranger's 'aqila.

Core Idea
A negligent leader's 'aqila pays diyah first, then recovers from the one who caused the danger.

This lesson examines a scenario from the Hidayah where a stranger ties his camel to a caravan without the leader's knowledge, and the camel kills someone. The Hanafi school holds the leader's 'aqila initially liable due to negligence, but grants them recourse against the stranger's 'aqila. Understanding this ruling clarifies the distinction between direct and indirect causation in diyat cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Recognize the leader (qa'id) who leads the caravan, the stranger (rabit) who ties his camel, and the victim. The act in question is the stranger tying his camel without permission, and the harm is the camel killing a person.
2
Determine initial liability
The leader's 'aqila (clan or insurance group) is liable for the diyah (blood money) because the leader could have prevented the tying by being vigilant. His failure to protect the caravan constitutes transgression (ta'addi), making him a cause (sabab) with liability.
3
Analyze the causation chain
The text explains that leading (al-qawd) is more directly connected to the loss than tying (al-ribt). Tying is like indirect causation (tasbib) while leading is like direct causation (mubashara). Hence the leader bears initial responsibility.
This is a qiyas: comparing the relationship between rabt and qawd to tasbib and mubashara.
4
Understand recourse
After paying, the leader's 'aqila can recover the diyah from the stranger's 'aqila, because the stranger is the one who involved them in this liability. Although both are causes, the stranger's act (tying) is the root cause.
5
Apply the principle
The general principle: when multiple causers exist, the one whose act is more directly connected to the harm bears initial liability, but ultimate burden shifts to the primary cause if they were negligent or intentional.
This ruling is an example of ta'addi (transgression) converting a mere cause into a liable party.

کسی شخص نے اونٹ قطار سے باندھ دیا اور قائد کواس کا علم نہیں اور مربوط نے کسی کو روند کر ہلاک کر دیا قائد کے عاقلہ پر دیت ہے
قال وإن ربط رجل بعيرا إلى القطار والقائد لا يعلم فوطىء المربوط إنسانا فقتله فعلى عاقلة القائد الدية لأنه يمكنه صيانة القطار عن ربط غيره فإذا ترك الصيانة صار متعديا وفي التسبيب الدية على العاقلة كما في القتل الخطأ
ترجمہ مصنف نے فرمایا اور اگر کسی شخص نے اونٹ قطار سے باندھ دیا اور قائد کو اس کا علم نہیں ہے پس مربوط نے (جس کو باندھا گیا ہے ) کسی انسان کو روند کر ہلاک کر دیا تو قائد کے عاقلہ پر دیت ہے اس لئے کہ اس کو قطار کی حفاظت اپنے غیر کے باندھنے سے ممکن تھی پس جب اس نے حفاظت کو چھوڑ دیا تو وہ متعدی ہو گیا اور تسبیب میں دیت عاقلہ پر ہوتی ہے، جیسے قتل خطاء کے اندر۔ تشریح زید اپنی قطار لئے جارہا ہے خالد نے زید کی لاعلمی میں اپنا اونٹ بھی اسی میں لگا دیا اور خالد کے اونٹ نے کسی شخص کو ہلاک کر دیا تو زید کے عاقلہ پر اس کی دیت واجب ہوگی۔ کیونکہ زید تا غافل کیوں رہا اگر وہ چوکس رہتا تو اس کی لاعلمی میں خالد یہ حرکت نہ کر پاتا تو اس وجہ سے زید کی جانب تعدی ہوگئی اگر چہ وہ سبب ہے لیکن بصورت تعدی سبب کے اوپر ضمان واجب ہوتا ہے۔ عاقلہ پر دیت اس لئے واجب ہے کہ سبب کی صورت میں دیت عاقلہ پر ہی واجب ہوتی ہے جیسے قتل خطاء میں عاقلہ پر واجب ہوتی ہے۔
قائد سے عاقلہ رابطہ کے عاقلہ پر رجوع کریں
ثم يرجعون بها على عاقلة الرابط لأنه هو الذى أوقعهم في هذه العهدة وإنما لا يجب الضمان عليهما فى الابتداء وكل منهما مسبب لأن الربط من القود بمنزلة التسبيب من المباشرة لاتصال التلف بالقود دون الربط
ترجمہ پھر یہ زید کا عاقلہ ) رابط (خالد) کے عاقلہ پر رجوع کریں گے ۔ اس لئے کہ یہی ( خالد ) وہ ہے جس نے ان کو ( زید کے عاقلہ کو ) اس ذمہ داری میں پھنسایا ہے اور ضمان ان دونوں پر شروع ہی سے واجب نہیں ہے حالانکہ ان میں سے ہر ایک مسبب ہے اسلئے کہ باندھنا قود کے مقابلہ میں ایسا ہے جیسے تسبیب ہے مباشرت کے مقابلہ میں تلف کے متصل ہونے کی وجہ سے قود کے ساتھ نہ کہ ربط کے ساتھ۔
تشریح پہلی صورت میں زید کے عاقلہ پر ضمان واجب ہو گیا تھا لیکن زید کی برادری اس ضمان کو خالد کی مددگار برادری سے وصول
Someone tied a camel to the caravan and the leader does not know, and the tied camel trampled and killed someone. The blood money is on the leader's 'aqila. He said: And if a man ties a camel to the caravan and the leader does not know, and the tied camel tramples a man and kills him, then the blood money is on the leader's 'aqila, because he can protect the caravan from being tied by others. When he leaves protection, he becomes a transgressor, and in causation the blood money is on the 'aqila, as in accidental killing. Explanation: Zayd is going with his caravan. Khalid, without Zayd's knowledge, attaches his camel to it. Khalid's camel kills a person. So the diyah is wajib on Zayd's 'aqila. Because Zayd was negligent; if he had been vigilant, Khalid could not have done this. Thus, Zayd became a transgressor. Although he is a sabab, liability is obligatory on a sabab with transgression. Diyah is wajib on the 'aqila because in causation, diyah is on the 'aqila as in accidental killing. Then they (Zayd's 'aqila) return (collect) it from the 'aqila of the one who tied (Khalid). Because he is the one who involved them in this liability. And liability is not wajib on both initially, although each is a cause, because tying is in relation to leading like causation is to direct action, due to the loss being connected to leading, not to tying. Explanation: In the first case, liability was wajib on Zayd's 'aqila, but Zayd's clan will recover this liability from Khalid's assisting clan.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where a stranger ties his camel to a passing caravan without the leader's knowledge, and that camel causes a death. The ruling is that the leader's 'aqila is still liable for the diyah, because the leader had the ability to protect his caravan by being vigilant; his negligence constitutes transgression (ta'addi), making him liable as a cause (sabab). However, the leader's 'aqila can then recover the amount from the stranger's 'aqila, since the stranger is the one who actually created the dangerous situation. The reasoning is that the stranger's act of tying (al-ribt) is analogous to indirect causation (tasbib), while the leader's act of leading (al-qawd) is more like direct causation (mubashara) because the loss is directly connected to the leading. Therefore, the leader's 'aqila pays initially but can seek reimbursement. This illustrates the Hanafi principle of recourse among multiple causers.

Key Terms
رابط (rabit): The person who ties his camel to another's caravan, causing a dangerous situation.
قود (qawd): Leading the caravan, considered a more direct form of causation (mubashara) than tying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

After the leader's 'aqila pays the diyah for the camel incident, from whom do they recover the amount?
A) The victim's family
B) The stranger's 'aqila
C) The government treasury
D) No one, they bear the loss

Key Takeaway
A negligent leader's 'aqila pays diyah first but has recourse against the stranger's 'aqila, balancing victim compensation and ultimate accountability.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
10 min
Diyat Liability for a Camel Tied to a Caravan Without Leader's Knowledge
Explains the diyat liability and recovery between aqilas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on diyat liability when a camel tied to a caravan causes death, and identify the roles of direct and indirect causation (mubashir vs musabbib) in determining initial liability and recourse.

Core Idea
When direct and indirect causes concur, liability falls on the direct cause first, then the indirect cause bears ultimate responsibility.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a specific scenario in diyat law: a camel tied to a caravan without the leader's knowledge tramples a person to death. The Hanafi school distinguishes between direct and indirect causation to allocate blood money liability. In this lesson, we will dissect the ruling, the proof by analogy (qiyas) to accidental killing, and the mechanism of recourse between the 'aqilas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their acts
The leader (qa'id) drives the caravan. The tier (rabit) ties his camel to the caravan without permission. The victim is trampled by the tied camel.
The leader is considered the direct cause (mubashir) of the movement; the tier is an indirect cause (musabbib).
2
Determine initial liability
The diyat is initially wajib upon the leader's 'aqila because he failed to protect the caravan from unauthorized tying. His negligence makes him a transgressor (muta'addi).
The leader could have prevented the harm by being more vigilant.
3
Understand the legal reasoning (qiyas)
The ruling is analogized to accidental killing (qatl al-khata'), where the 'aqila bears the diyat. Here, the leader's failure to supervise is a cause similar to an accidental act.
The Arabic phrase in the text: 'وفي التسبيب الدية على العاقلة كما في القتل الخطأ'.
4
Trace the recourse mechanism
After the leader's 'aqila pays, they recover the diyat from the tier's 'aqila because the tier's action caused the leader's 'aqila to be burdened.
The tier is the ultimate cause (musabbib) of the loss.
5
Clarify the initial non-liability of the tier
Even though both are involved in causation, initial liability is only on the direct cause. This follows the principle: when mubashir and musabbib concur, liability is on the mubashir.
The damage is directly connected to the leader's driving (qiyadah), not to the tying (rabt).

Source Text

He said: If a man ties a camel to the caravan while the leader does not know, and the tied camel tramples a person and kills him, then the diyah is upon the leader's 'aqilah because he could have protected the caravan from the tying of another. When he abandoned protection, he became a transgressor. In the case of causation, the diyah is upon the 'aqilah, as in accidental killing. (Translation continues with Urdu commentary as in source.)
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school on liability when an animal tied to a caravan causes a death. The ruling states that the leader (qa'id) of the caravan is initially liable for diyat (blood money) on behalf of his 'aqila (tribal surety) because he failed in his duty to supervise and prevent unauthorized tying. The reasoning is that the leader is a direct cause (mubashir) of the harm by moving the caravan, whereas the tier (rabit) is an indirect cause (musabbib). In Hanafi fiqh, when a direct cause and indirect cause concur, liability falls on the direct cause first. However, the 'aqila of the leader can then recover the paid diyat from the 'aqila of the tier, since the tier's action caused the situation. The passage also addresses a potential question: why not hold both liable initially? The answer explains that liability is placed only on the direct cause (the leader) at first; the tier bears ultimate responsibility but only after recovery. This distinction is based on the principle that the immediate cause of death is the movement (qiyadah) of the camel, not the tying (rabt).

Key Terms
القطار (al-qitar): A line of camels tied together in a caravan.
القائد (al-qa'id): The leader who drives the caravan.
الرابط (al-rabiṭ): The person who ties his camel to a caravan without permission.
العاقلة (al-'āqilah): The agnatic group of a person who pays blood money on his behalf.
التسبيب (al-tasbīb): Causation; indirect involvement leading to harm.
المباشرة (al-mubāsharah): Direct commission of an act causing harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a camel is tied to a caravan without the leader's knowledge and causes a death, who bears the initial responsibility for diyat?
A) The owner of the camel (the tier)
B) The leader of the caravan
C) Both equally
D) Neither, the animal's owner only

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi diyat law, when a direct cause and indirect cause concur, the direct cause bears initial liability but can recover from the indirect cause.

Diyat — Blood Money

Liability for Diyat: Tying While Moving vs. Stationary
Clarifies liability based on timing of tying and implicit permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when diyat liability falls on the tier (one who ties) versus the caravan leader based on timing of tying, and apply the concept of implicit permission (dalalah) to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
Implicit permission during movement alters liability; stationary tying without consent shifts liability to the leader.

In this passage, the mashayikh refine a prior ruling from the Hidayah by introducing a critical temporal distinction. The key factor is whether the animal was tied while the caravan was already moving (implicitly commanding the leader to drive) or while it was stationary (no permission granted). This distinction determines who bears ultimate responsibility for diyat if the animal causes harm. Understanding this nuance is essential for correctly assigning liability in caravan accidents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct situations: (a) a camel is tied while the caravan is moving, and (b) a camel is tied while the caravan is stationary and then the leader moves it. Identify what differs between them.
The timing of the tying relative to movement is decisive.
2
Understand the concept of dalalah (implicit permission)
In scenario (a), tying while moving is treated as an implicit command to the leader to drive the camel. This is based on the Hanafi principle that actions can indicate consent (dalalah).
Dalalah is contrasted with sarih (explicit) permission.
3
Trace the liability allocation
In scenario (a), because the leader cannot protect himself from harm due to unawareness, liability rests on the tier. In scenario (b), the leader moves a camel without any permission (neither explicit nor implicit), so he bears full liability and cannot recover from the tier.
The leader's knowledge and ability to prevent harm are key factors.
4
Note the ruling's basis (talil)
The reasoning (talil) is based on the presence or absence of implicit permission. The mashayikh explain that in scenario (a) the tier implicitly authorized the drive, while in scenario (b) no authorization exists.
This is a qiyas-like extension from the principle of permission.
5
Recognize the cross-madhhab dimension
Other schools may not accept the concept of implicit permission (dalalah) as establishing liability. The passage notes that this distinction is specific to the Hanafi school and is mukhtalaf fih.
Understanding this ikhtilaf helps in comparative fiqh discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کسی نے اپنا چوپایہ قطار میں باندھ دیا قائد کو اس کا علم نہ ہوا تو ضامن رابط ہوگا
قالوا هذا ربط والقطار تسير لأنه أمر بالقود دلالة فإذا لم يعلم به لا يمكنه التحفظ من ذالك فيكون قرار الضمان على الرابط أما إذا ربط والإبل قيام ثم قادها ضمنها القائد لأنه قاد بعير غيره بغير إذنه لا صريحا ولا دلالة فلا يرجع بما لحقه عليه.
ترجمہ مشائخ نے فرمایا یہ اس وقت ہے جب کہ اس نے باندھا ہو حالانکہ قطار چل رہی ہو اس لئے کہ وہ دلالت قود کا حکم دینے والا ہے پس جب کہ اس نے (قائد نے ) اس کو ( باندھنے کو نہیں جانا تو قائد کو اس سے تحفظ ممکن نہیں ہے تو ضمان کا قرار ( ٹھہراؤ) رابط پر ہوگا۔ بہر حال جب کہ اس نے باندھا ہو حالانکہ اونٹ کھڑے ہیں پھر اس نے ان کو کھینچا تو دیت کا ضامن قائد ہو گا اس لئے کہ قائد نے اپنے غیر کے اونٹ کو کھینچا ہے بغیر اس کی اجازت کے جو نہ صراحت ہے اور نہ دلالہ ، پس قائد اس ضمان میں جو اس کو لاحق ہوا ہے رابط پر رجوع نہیں کرے گا۔ تشریح زید نے عاقلہ کو خالد کے عاقلہ سے دیت واپس لینے کا اختیار صرف اس وقت ہے جب کہ زید اپنی قطار سے جاتا ہے اور خالد نے اپنا اونٹ جوڑ دیا ہے کیونکہ اس صورت میں گویا دلالہ خالد زید کو یہ حکم کر رہا ہے کہ میرا اونٹ کھینچو اور چونکہ زید کو اس کا علم نہیں کہ وہ حفاظت کر سکے۔ اس لئے زید کو معذور قرار دے کر آخر کار خالد کے عاقلہ پر ضمان واجب ہو جائے گا ( کمامر ) ۔ اور اگر خالد نے اپنا اونٹ اس حال میں باندھا ہو کہ اونٹ کھڑے ہوں اور پھر زیدان کو لے کر چلا ہو اور خالد کے اونٹ نے کسی کو ہلاک کر دیا ہو تو یہاں زید کے عاقلہ پر دیت کا ضمان ہوگا اور یہ لوگ اس کو خالد کے عاقلہ سے واپس لینے کے حقدار نہ ہوں گے کیونکہ یہاں خالد کی جانب سے اپنے اونٹ کو کھینچنے کی کسی طرح کی اجازت نہیں پائی گئی تو زید ہی مجرم نہرا تو مضان کا قرار بھی اسی پر ہوگا۔
Someone tied his animal to the caravan and the leader did not know, so the guarantor is the tier. They said: This is when he tied while the caravan was moving, because he gives a command for driving by implication. When he (the leader) does not know, he cannot protect from that, so the liability rests on the tier. But if he tied while the camels were standing, then he led them, the leader is liable for diyat because he drove another's camel without his permission, neither explicitly nor implicitly, so he cannot recover from the tier for what befell him. (Urdu commentary follows.)
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage refines the previous ruling by introducing a temporal distinction. The mashayikh (teachers) clarify that the initial ruling (where the leader can recover from the tier) applies only when the tier ties his camel while the caravan is already moving. In that case, the tier's act implicitly commands the leader to drive (dalalat al-qiyadah), and the leader cannot prevent it due to being unaware, so the tier bears ultimate liability. However, if the tier ties his camel while the caravan is stationary, and then the leader later moves it, the leader is directly liable because he drove another's camel without any permission—neither explicit nor implied. Therefore, the leader cannot recover from the tier. This distinction hinges on the concept of 'dalalah' (implicit permission) versus direct action without authority.

Key Terms
دلالة (dalālah): Implicit indication or permission derived from action or context.
قود (qawd): Driving the caravan; the act of leading the camels.
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee, here specifically diyat (blood money).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the leader of a caravan become directly liable for diyat rather than the tier?
A) When the tier ties his camel while the caravan is moving
B) When the tier ties his camel while the caravan is stationary and the leader then moves it
C) Always the tier is liable
D) Always the leader is liable

Key Takeaway
The presence or absence of implicit permission (dalālah) determines whether the tier or leader bears diyat liability when a tied animal causes harm during caravan travel.

Driver's Liability for Immediate Animal Attack
States the driver's liability with a causal reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on driver liability when a released animal immediately attacks someone, and explain why the driver (not the owner) is the direct cause.

Core Idea
The driver is liable because his driving directly causes the animal's immediate attack.

When an animal is released and driven, and it immediately attacks a person, who bears the blood money? This passage from Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani) clarifies that the driver (sā'iq) is liable, not the owner. The reasoning is that the act of killing is transferred to the driver through his driving. Let's walk through the ruling and its underlying principle of causation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
First, note the hukm: the driver (sā'iq) is liable for diyat. This is a wajib upon him or his ʻāqila.
2
Identify the Condition
The passage specifies that the attack occurs 'immediately' (fawr). This condition is crucial: it links the driver's action directly to the harm without any intervening cause.
If the attack is delayed, liability may shift.
3
Understand the Causal Link
The reason given is that the act of killing is transferred to the driver by means of his driving (sawq). In Hanafi law, the driver is the mubāshir (direct cause) because he controls the animal's movement.
Contrast with the animal's owner, who may be a mutasabbib (indirect cause) and not immediately liable.
4
Recognize the Legal Principle
This case exemplifies the principle that the direct cause (mubāshir) bears liability when the consequence is immediate. The driving act makes the driver the proximate cause of the attack.
5
Note Cross-Madhhab Disagreement
The Hanafi position is mukhtalaf fīh. Other schools might hold the owner liable if the animal was not under proper control. Understand the practical difference (thamarah).
The muftā bihī in the Hanafi school is that only the driver is liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کسی نے چو پایا چھوڑا اور اس کا ہانکنے والا تھا چوپائے نے کسی پر حملہ کر کے اسے ہلاک کر دیا سائق پر ضمان ہے
قال ومن أرسل بهيمة وكان لها سائقا فأصابت فى فورها يضمنه لأن الفعل انتقل إليه بواسطة السوق
ترجمہ محمد نے فرمایا اور جس نے چوپایہ چھوڑا اور وہ اس کا ہانکنے والا تھا پس وہ فورا کسی پر حملہ کر بیٹھا تو سائق پر اس کا ضمان ہو گا۔ اس
Someone released an animal and was its driver; the animal attacked someone and killed him. The driver is liable. He (Imam Muhammad) said: Whoever releases an animal and is its driver, and it immediately strikes, he is liable for it because the act transferred to him by means of the driving. Translation: Muhammad said: And whoever released an animal and he was its driver, and it immediately attacked someone, then the driver is liable for it. ...
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Imam Muhammad (one of the Sahibayn) regarding an animal that is released and immediately attacks a person, causing death. The ruling states that the driver (sa'iq) is liable for diyat. The reason given is that the act of killing is transferred to the driver through his action of driving the animal. In Hanafi jurisprudence, this is an application of the principle that the direct cause (here, driving) brings liability when the consequence is immediate. The Urdu commentary clarifies that the liability falls on the driver, not the owner of the animal, because the driver's actions precipitated the attack.

Key Terms
سائق (sā'iq): The driver who drives the animal.
فور (fawr): Immediate, without delay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who is liable when a person releases an animal, drives it, and the animal immediately kills someone?
A) The owner of the animal
B) The driver (sa'iq)
C) Both owner and driver equally
D) No one, the animal acted on its own

Key Takeaway
The driver of an animal that immediately attacks is liable for diyat because his driving is the direct cause of the harm.

Liability for Tying an Animal in a Caravan: Tier vs. Leader
Ruling with proof on liability in diyat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish two scenarios of tying an animal in a caravan and state who bears liability for blood-money (diyah) based on the presence or absence of implied permission (dalalah).

Core Idea
When tying an animal in a moving caravan, the tier is liable; when tying stationary, the leader is liable — the difference is implied permission.

This passage from the Hidayah clarifies a nuanced rule in Hanafi tort law: the liability for an animal that causes harm while part of a caravan. The key is whether the leader (qa'id) was aware of the animal being tied. If the tie occurs while the caravan is moving, the tier (rabit) is considered to have implicitly commanded the leader, shifting liability to the tier. Conversely, if the animal is tied while stationary and then driven, the leader acts without any permission and bears full liability. Understanding this distinction requires careful application of the Hanafi principles of causation (tasabbub) versus direct action (mubasharah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct factual patterns: (1) an animal tied into a moving caravan without the leader's knowledge, and (2) an animal tied while the caravan is stationary, then led by the leader.
Each scenario has a different liable party.
2
Understand the role of dalalah (implied permission)
Tying into a moving caravan is considered an implied command (dalalah) to the leader to drive the animal. Thus, the leader is not acting without permission.
Dalalah is a recognized source of legal implication in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Apply the distinction between mubashir and sabab
In the first scenario, the leader is the direct actor (mubashir) but the harm is attributed to the tier (sabab) due to the implied command. In the second, the leader acts without any permission, so he is solely liable as the direct perpetrator.
This follows the Hanafi rule that a direct actor without permission bears full liability.
4
Determine the practical consequence: right of recourse
The passage explains that the leader's 'aqila can reclaim blood-money from the tier's 'aqila only in the first scenario. In the second, the leader's 'aqila has no recourse.
This shows the legal effect of the liability assignment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کسی نے اپنا چوپایہ قطار میں باندھ دیا قائد کو اس کا علم نہ ہوا تو ضامن رابط ہوگا
قالوا هذا ربط والقطار تسير لأنه أمر بالقود دلالة فإذا لم يعلم به لا يمكنه التحفظ من ذالك فيكون قرار الضمان على الرابط أما إذا ربط والإبل قيام ثم قادها ضمنها القائد لأنه قاد بعير غيره بغير إذنه لا صريحا ولا دلالة فلا يرجع بما لحقه عليه.
ترجمہ مشائخ نے فرمایا یہ اس وقت ہے جب کہ اس نے باندھا ہو حالانکہ قطار چل رہی ہو اس لئے کہ وہ دلالت قود کا حکم دینے والا ہے پس جب کہ اس نے (قائد نے ) اس کو ( باندھنے کو نہیں جانا تو قائد کو اس سے تحفظ ممکن نہیں ہے تو ضمان کا قرار ( ٹھہراؤ) رابط پر ہوگا۔ بہر حال جب کہ اس نے باندھا ہو حالانکہ اونٹ کھڑے ہیں پھر اس نے ان کو کھینچا تو دیت کا ضامن قائد ہو گا اس لئے کہ قائد نے اپنے غیر کے اونٹ کو کھینچا ہے بغیر اس کی اجازت کے جو نہ صراحت ہے اور نہ دلالہ ، پس قائد اس ضمان میں جو اس کو لاحق ہوا ہے رابط پر رجوع نہیں کرے گا۔
تشریح زید نے عاقلہ کو خالد کے عاقلہ سے دیت واپس لینے کا اختیار صرف اس وقت ہے جب کہ زید اپنی قطار سے جاتا ہے اور خالد نے اپنا اونٹ جوڑ دیا ہے کیونکہ اس صورت میں گویا دلالہ خالد زید کو یہ حکم کر رہا ہے کہ میرا اونٹ کھینچو اور چونکہ زید کو اس کا علم نہیں کہ وہ حفاظت کر سکے۔ اس لئے زید کو معذور قرار دے کر آخر کار خالد کے عاقلہ پر ضمان واجب ہو جائے گا ( کمامر ) ۔ اور اگر خالد نے اپنا اونٹ اس حال میں باندھا ہو کہ اونٹ کھڑے ہوں اور پھر زیدان کو لے کر چلا ہو اور خالد کے اونٹ نے کسی کو ہلاک کر دیا ہو تو یہاں زید کے عاقلہ پر دیت کا ضمان ہوگا اور یہ لوگ اس کو خالد کے عاقلہ سے واپس لینے کے حقدار نہ ہوں گے کیونکہ یہاں خالد کی جانب سے اپنے اونٹ کو کھینچنے کی کسی طرح کی اجازت نہیں پائی گئی تو زید ہی مجرم [؟] تو ضمان کا قرار بھی اسی پر ہوگا۔
Someone tied his animal in a caravan while the leader was unaware, so the tier (rabit) is the guarantor. They (the mashayikh) said: This is when he tied while the caravan was moving, because he (the tier) is, by indication (dalalah), commanding the leader to drive. So when the leader did not know of it, he cannot protect against it, so the liability rests on the tier. But if he tied while the camels were standing, then later he led them, the leader is liable for the blood-money because he led another's camel without permission, neither explicit nor by indication, so he cannot refer back to the tier for what befell him. Explanation: Zayd's 'aqila has the right to reclaim the blood-money from Khalid's 'aqila only when Zayd is proceeding with his own caravan and Khalid attached his camel, because in that case Khalid is, by indication, commanding Zayd to pull his camel, and since Zayd did not know to take precaution, Zayd is deemed excused and ultimately liability falls on Khalid's 'aqila. But if Khalid tied his camel while the camels were standing and then Zayd drove them and Khalid's camel killed someone, then Zayd's 'aqila bears the blood-money and they have no right to reclaim it from Khalid's 'aqila because there was no permission from Khalid to pull his camel, so Zayd is the criminal and liability rests on him.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage delivers a ruling from the Hanafi school on the liability for an animal that causes harm when tied in a moving caravan (qatar). The passage begins with a ruling statement: if someone ties his animal into a caravan without the leader's knowledge, the tier (rabit) is liable, not the leader. The proof is provided by the mashayikh: tying in a moving caravan implies an instruction to the leader to drive the animal, so the leader cannot be blamed for the accident. However, if the animal was tied while stationary and then the leader drove it, the leader becomes the direct perpetrator (mubashir) and bears liability because he acted without any permission. The passage then explains a practical application: Zayd's 'aqila can reclaim blood-money from Khalid's 'aqila only in the first scenario (tier's implied permission). In the second scenario, Zayd's 'aqila must bear the loss. The reasoning hinges on the distinction between causation (tasabbub) and direct action (mubasharah): when the tier ties in a moving caravan, the leader is the direct actor but the harm is attributed to the tier due to implied command; when the animal is tied stationary, the leader's direct action without permission makes him solely liable.

Key Terms
رابط (rabit): The person who ties an animal into a caravan (qatar). The rabit is considered a cause (sabab) of the animal's movement.
قائد (qa'id): The person leading or driving a caravan. The qa'id is the direct actor (mubashir) in moving the animal.
دلالة (dalalah): Implied indication or consent. In this context, tying an animal in a moving caravan serves as an implied command to the leader to drive it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where Khalid ties his camel to Zayd's moving caravan without Zayd's knowledge, and the camel kills someone, who is liable for the blood-money?
A) Zayd's 'aqila
B) Khalid's 'aqila
C) Both 'aqilas equally
D) Neither, the victim bears the loss

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between tying in a moving caravan versus tying stationary rests on the presence of implied permission (dalalah), which shifts liability from the direct actor (leader) to the cause (tier).

Liability of the Driver for Harm by a Released Animal: Quadrupeds vs. Birds
Hanafi ruling on liability of the driver for harm caused by a released quadruped vs. bird.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for harm caused by a driven quadruped versus a driven bird, and explain the legal basis for the distinction based on physical capacity for driving.

Core Idea
Driving a quadruped transfers liability to the driver; driving a bird is legally irrelevant because a bird cannot be effectively driven.

In Hanafi fiqh, when an animal is released by a driver and immediately causes harm, liability depends on whether the animal's body can physically accommodate being driven. This passage from Imam Muhammad illustrates the key distinction between quadrupeds (such as dogs) and birds (such as falcons). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly attributing acts in diyat (injuries) cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is releasing an animal while being its driver, and the animal immediately causes harm. The ruling: liability (daman) on the driver.
The ruling is from Imam Muhammad in Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
The passage gives two distinct cases: a quadruped (e.g., dog) and a bird (e.g., falcon). For the quadruped, the driver is liable; for the bird, he is not.
3
Locate the ratio (talil)
The reason for the distinction is given: 'the body of a quadruped can withstand driving (sawq), so his driving is considered; a bird cannot withstand driving, so the existence and non-existence of driving are equal.'
This ratio is based on the physical capacity of the animal.
4
Apply the principle
If the animal's body can accommodate actual driving (haqiqi), then the act is attributed to the driver, making him liable. If the animal cannot be driven effectively, the driving is nominal only and does not transfer attribution.
Attribution of the animal's act to the releaser depends on the realization of driving.
5
Note the summary
The passage concludes: 'The basis for attributing to the releaser is driving (sawq) and its realization.'
This is a general principle for cases of this kind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کسی نے چو پایا چھوڑا اور اس کا ہانکنے والا تھا چوپائے نے کسی پر حملہ کر کے اسے ہلاک کر دیا سائق پر ضمان ہے
قال ومن أرسل بهيمة وكان لها سائقا فأصابت فى فورها يضمنه لأن الفعل انتقل إليه بواسطة السوق
ترجمہ محمد نے فرمایا اور جس نے چوپایہ چھوڑا اور وہ اس کا ہانکنے والا تھا پس وہ فورا کسی پر حملہ کر بیٹھا تو سائق پر اس کا ضمان ہو گا۔ اس لئے کہ فعل سوق کے واسطہ سے اسی کی جانب منتقل ہوگا۔
تشریح زید نے مثلاً کتا چھوڑا اور وہ پہلے اس کا سائق تھا اس نے کسی کو مارڈالا تو زید اس کا ضامن ہوگا۔ کیونکہ سوق کے واسطہ سے کتنے کا فعل اسی کی جانب منسوب ہوگا۔
پرندہ (باز ) چھوڑا اور اس کو ہانگا، اس پرندے نے کسی مملوک شکار کو قتل کر دیا ضامن نہ ہوگا
قال ولو أرسل طيرًا وساقه فأصاب فى فوره لم يضمن والفرق أن بدن البهيمة يحتمل السوق فاعتبر سوقه والطير لا يحتمل السوق فصار وجود السوق وعدمه بمنزلة
ترجمہ محمد نے فرمایا اور اگر پرندہ (باز ) چھوڑا اور اس کو ہانکا پس اس نے فوراً ( کسی کی مملوک شکار کو قتل کر دیا تو وہ ضامن نہ ہو گا اور فرق یہ ہے کہ چوپائے کا بدن سوق کا احتمال رکھتا ہے تو اس کا سوق معتبر ہوا اور پرندہ سوق کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا تو سوق کا وجود اور اس کا عدم ایک درجہ میں ہو گیا۔
تشریح کتا چونکہ چو پایہ ہے جس کو ہانکا جا سکتا ہے اور پرندہ کونہیں ہانکا جاسکتا تو کتے کو ہانکنا معتبر ہوگا اور فعل کی نسبت سوق کی وجہ سے مرسل کی جانب کر دی جائے گی اور اگر باز کو ہانکا تو وہ نہ ہانکنے کے درجہ میں ہے، لہذا باز کو ہانکنے کی وجہ سے فعل کی نسبت مرسل کی جانب نہ ہوگی اور ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا۔
خلاصہ کلام ... مرسل کی طرف نسبت کا مدار سوق اور اس کے تحقق پر ہے۔
Someone released a quadruped and had a driver for it; the quadruped attacked and killed someone; the driver is liable. Muhammad said: And whoever released a quadruped and had a driver for it, and it immediately struck, he is liable because the act is transferred to him through the driving. Explanation: For example, Zayd released a dog and he was its driver; it killed someone; Zayd is liable because the dog's act is attributed to him through the driving. (As for) a bird (falcon) released and driven, if it killed someone's owned catch, he is not liable. Muhammad said: And if he released a bird and drove it, and it immediately struck, he is not liable, and the difference is that the body of a quadruped can withstand driving, so his driving is considered; a bird cannot withstand driving, so the existence and non-existence of driving are equal. Explanation: Since the dog is a quadruped that can be driven, driving it is effective and the act is attributed to the releaser. But if he drove a falcon, it is as if he did not drive it, so the act is not attributed to the releaser and liability does not arise. Summary: The basis for attributing to the releaser is driving (sawq) and its realization.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings from Imam Muhammad regarding liability when an animal is released with a driver. First, for a quadruped (like a dog) that can be physically driven, if the driver (sa'iq) releases it and it immediately causes harm, the driver bears liability because the act is attributed to him via the driving. Second, for a bird (like a falcon) that cannot be effectively driven, even if the driver makes driving motions, it is legally equivalent to no driving; thus, the act is not attributed to the releaser, and no liability arises. The key distinction is the nature of the animal: a quadruped's body can withstand driving, so driving is real and effective; a bird's body does not, so driving is nominal only. The text concludes that attribution of the animal's act to the releaser depends entirely on actual driving (sawq). All examples are from Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
سائق (sa'iq): The person who drives (sawq) an animal, i.e., actively moves it along. In this context, the sa'iq is the party whose driving attributes the animal's act to him.
سوق (sawq): The act of driving an animal. Sawq is effective only if the animal's body can physically accommodate it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the driver (sa'iq) not liable when he releases and drives a falcon that kills someone's game?
A) Because falcons are not owned animals
B) Because falcons have free will and cannot be controlled
C) Because the body of a falcon cannot withstand driving, so driving is legally irrelevant
D) Because the victim's game is not a human being

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between quadrupeds and birds for liability of a driver: driving is effective only if the animal's body can physically accommodate it.

Dog Without a Driver: No Liability, Exception for Hunting
Ruling on liability for a dog released without a driver, with exception for hunting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for a dog released without a driver and explain the exception for hunting based on the principle of necessity (hajah).

Core Idea
A dog's act is not attributed to its releaser, except for hunting where necessity makes the game halal.

In this passage, the Hidayah concludes the discussion of liability for animals by addressing a dog released without a driver. The general rule is that an animal acts independently (mukhtar) and cannot serve as a deputy (na'ib) for its owner, so its harmful acts do not incur liability. However, a special exception exists for hunting: because hunting is a permissible activity that can only be achieved through the dog's action, the dog's catch is attributed to the releaser, making the game halal. This lesson explores the legal reasoning behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between: (1) a dog without a driver that causes harm (e.g., kills a person) and (2) a dog without a driver that catches game. In the first, there is no liability; in the second, the game is permissible.
2
State the general rule and its basis
The general rule is that no liability (daman) is incurred for the dog's harmful act because the dog is 'mukhtar' (independent in action) and not a 'na'ib' (deputy) for the releaser. Thus, the act is not attributed to the releaser.
This relies on the principle that animals are not agents for humans in general.
3
Identify the exception for hunting
For hunting, even though the dog has no driver, its act of catching and killing game is attributed to the releaser, making the game halal (as if the releaser slaughtered it).
4
Analyze the legal reasoning (talil)
The difference is based on necessity (hajah): hunting is a legitimate activity (mubah) and there is no other way to achieve it except through the dog's action. Therefore, the act is attributed to the releaser out of necessity. For aggression (udwan), there is no such necessity, so the general rule applies.
This shows that necessity can override a general legal principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کتا چھوڑا اور اس کا کوئی سائق نہیں تھا کوئی ضامن نہیں ہوگا
وكذا لو أرسل كلبا ولم يكن له سائقا لم يضمن ولو أرسله إلى صيد ولم يكن له سائقا فأخذ الصيد وقتله حل ووجه الفرق أن البهيمة مختارة فى فعلها ولا تصلح نائبة عن المرسل فلايضاف فعلها إلى غيرها هذا هو الحقيقة إلا أن الحاجة مست في الاصطياد فأضيف إلى المرسل لأن الاصطياد مشروع ولا طريق له سواه ولا حاجة في حق ضمان العدوان
ترجمہ اور ایسے ہی اگر اس نے کتا چھوڑا اور وہ اس کا سائق نہیں تھا تو ضامن نہ ہوگا اور اگر کتے کو شکار پر چھوڑا اور اس کا سائق نہیں تھا۔ پس اس نے شکار پکڑ لیا اور اس کو قتل کر دیا تو حلال ہے، اور وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ چوپایہ اپنے فعل میں مختار ہے اور وہ مرسل کا نائب بننے کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتا تو چوپائے کا فعل اس کے غیر کی جانب مضاف نہ ہو گا۔ یہی حقیقت ہے مگر تحقیق حاجت پیش آتی ہے اصطیاد کی تو اصطیاد مرسل کی جانب مضاف ہوگا اس لئے کہ اصطیاد مباح ہے اور شکار کا اس کے علاوہ ( کہ کتے کا فعل مرسل کی جانب منسوب ہو ) کوئی طریقہ نہیں ہے اور تعدی کے ضمان کے حق میں کوئی حاجت نہیں ہے۔
تشریح اگر کتے کو چھوڑ دیا اور اس کو ہانکا نہیں اور اس نے کسی کو ہلاک کر دیا تو مرسل پر ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ کتے کا فعل مرسل کی طرف مضاف نہ ہوگا۔ اور اگر کتے کو شکار پر چھوڑا ہو اور اس نے شکار کو پکڑ کر ہلاک کر دیا تو یہاں کتے کا فعل مرسل کی طرف مضاف ہوگا۔ اور ایسا سمجھیں گے گویا مرسل ہی نے شکار کو ذبح کر دیا ہے لہذا شکار حلال ہوگا۔
If a dog is released and it had no driver, no one is liable. Similarly, if he released a dog and it had no driver, he is not liable. And if he released it toward game and it had no driver, and it caught and killed the game, it is permissible (halal). The difference is that the quadruped is independent in its action and is not fit to be a deputy for the releaser, so its action is not attributed to another. This is the reality. However, need arises in hunting, so (the act) is attributed to the releaser because hunting is permissible and there is no other way to achieve it. There is no such need in the case of liability for aggression. Explanation: If the dog was released without being driven, and it killed someone, the releaser is not liable because the dog's act is not attributed to him. But if the dog was released for hunting and it caught and killed the game, then the dog's act is attributed to the releaser, and it is as if the releaser slaughtered it, so the game becomes halal.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion of liability for animals by addressing the case of a dog released without a driver. The general rule is that the dog acts independently (mukhtarah) and cannot serve as a deputy (na'ib) for the releaser, so its harmful act is not attributed to the releaser, and no liability arises. However, an exception is made for hunting: because hunting is a legitimate activity (mubah) and the animal's action is the only means to achieve it, the dog's act of catching and killing game is legally attributed to the releaser, making the game permissible (halal) as if the releaser personally slaughtered it. This exception does not extend to cases of aggression (udwan) because there is no such need. The explanation emphasizes that the attribution for hunting is based on necessity (hajah), not on any general principle of agency. Thus, the same dog that causes no liability if it harms a person without a driver becomes an instrument of the releaser for the purpose of hunting.

Key Terms
نائب (na'ib): A deputy or representative. A quadruped (like a dog) cannot be a na'ib for a person in general actions, so its acts are not attributed to the releaser.
مختار (mukhtar): Independent in action. The animal acts by its own choice, so its act is not attributed to the releaser unless a specific legal cause exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is game caught by a dog without a driver considered halal (permissible) to eat?
A) Because the dog automatically acts as the owner's agent
B) Because hunting is a permissible activity and there is no other way to achieve it
C) Because the dog is trained and always obeys
D) Because the owner explicitly commands the dog

Key Takeaway
A dog without a driver acts independently, so its harmful acts do not incur liability, but its hunting catches are attributed to the releaser due to necessity, making the game halal.

Liability for Harm Caused by a Driven Animal: The Distinction Between Quadrupeds and Birds
Ruling on attribution of harm through driving (sāq)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when an animal is driven and kills or harms someone, and distinguish between cases based on the animal's capacity to be driven.

Core Idea
Liability attaches only when the animal can actually be driven; a bird's driving is legally irrelevant.

In Hanafi tort law (diyat), the key to attribution of an animal's act to its releaser is the concept of 'sawq' (driving). This passage from al-Hidayah clarifies that not all animals can be driven effectively. While a dog or a horse can be led and controlled, a bird cannot. This distinction determines whether the releaser bears liability. Let us unpack the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is releasing a driven animal. The ruling: if the animal is a quadruped and actually driven, the releaser is liable (wajib compensation). If the animal is a bird, no liability.
The passage uses a dog as the example of a quadruped.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the explicit distinction (farq) provided: 'بدن البهيمة يحتمل السوق والطير لا يحتمل السوق' (the body of a quadruped can sustain driving, the bird cannot).
This is a clear statement of the effective cause (illa).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the dalil prove the ruling? The driving is effective for quadrupeds, so the animal's act is attributed to the driver. For birds, driving is meaningless, so no attribution and no liability.
The phrase 'صار وجود السوق وعدمه بمنزلة' (driving and not driving become equivalent) is key.
4
Note the scope and ikhtilaf
This ruling applies only to animals that can be effectively driven. Imam Abu Yusuf differed and extended liability to all cases as a precaution; later fatwa followed his view.
The intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is important for practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

۲۴۳
تشریح زید نے مثلاً کتا چھوڑا اور وہ پہلے اس کا سائق تھا اس نے کسی کو مارڈالا تو زید اس کا ضامن ہوگا۔ کیونکہ سوق کے واسطہ سے کتنے کا فعل اسی کی جانب منسوب ہوگا۔
پرندہ (باز ) چھوڑا اور اس کو ہانگا، اس پرندے نے کسی مملوک شکار کو قتل کر دیا ضامن نہ ہوگا
کتاب الديات
قال ولو أرسل طيرًا وساقه فأصاب فى فوره لم يضمن والفرق أن بدن البهيمة يحتمل السوق فاعتبر سوقه والطير لا يحتمل السوق فصار وجود السوق وعدمه بمنزلة
ترجمہ محمد نے فرمایا اور اگر پرندہ (باز ) چھوڑا اور اس کو ہانکا پس اس نے فوراً ( کسی کی مملوک شکار کو قتل کر دیا تو وہ ضامن نہ ہو گا اور فرق یہ ہے کہ چوپائے کا بدن سوق کا احتمال رکھتا ہے تو اس کا سوق معتبر ہوا اور پرندہ سوق کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا تو سوق کا وجود اور اس کا عدم ایک درجہ میں ہو گیا۔

خلاصہ کلام ... مرسل کی طرف نسبت کا مدار سوق اور اس کے تحقق پر ہے۔
Because the act (of the animal) is transferred to him through the driving. Explanation: Zaid, for example, released a dog and he was its driver; it killed someone, so Zaid is liable for it. Because through the driving, the dog's act is attributed to him. He released a bird (falcon) and drove it; that bird killed someone's owned prey, he is not liable. Chapter of Blood Money. He said: If he released a bird and drove it, and it struck immediately, he is not liable. The difference is that the body of a quadruped can sustain driving, so his driving is considered; the bird cannot sustain driving, so the existence of driving and its absence are equivalent. Translation: Muhammad said: If he released a bird (falcon) and drove it, and it immediately killed someone's owned prey, he is not liable. The difference is that the body of a quadruped can sustain driving, so his driving is considered; the bird cannot sustain driving, so the existence of driving and its absence become equal. Explanation: Since a dog is a quadruped that can be driven, and a bird cannot be driven, driving the dog is considered, and the act is attributed to the sender due to the driving. If he drove a falcon, it is as if he did not drive it, so the act is not attributed to the sender and liability is not obligatory. Summary: The basis of attribution to the sender is the driving and its actualization.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on liability for harm caused by an animal that has been released or set loose. The key distinction is whether the animal is 'driven' (sāq) or not. The author first illustrates with a dog: if Zaid releases a dog while acting as its driver (sā'iq), and the dog kills someone, Zaid is liable because the dog's act is attributed to him through the driving. Then the case of a bird is given: if someone releases a falcon and drives it (tries to guide it), and it immediately kills someone's owned prey, the sender is not liable. The reason, as stated in the Arabic matn, is that the body of a quadruped (like a dog) can sustain driving (i.e., it can be led and controlled), whereas a bird cannot be driven effectively; thus, for a bird, the presence or absence of driving is legally irrelevant. The commentary explains that since a dog is a quadruped that can be driven, the act is attributed to the sender; but for a falcon, driving it is like not driving it, so no attribution and no liability. The summary reiterates that attribution depends on actual driving (sāq) and its realization.

Key Terms
سائق (sā'iq): The person who drives or guides an animal that is released, making its act attributable to him.
سوق (sawq): The act of driving or goading an animal to influence its movement.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to pay compensation for harm or damage caused.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reason a bird does not incur liability when driven and it kills someone's property?
A) Birds are not owned by anyone.
B) Birds cannot be effectively driven, so driving is irrelevant.
C) Birds are always lawful to kill.
D) The owner did not intend harm.

Key Takeaway
Liability for an animal's harm through driving requires that the animal be capable of being effectively driven; a bird's driving is irrelevant.
